Why does the human's obligatory artifice take, as its founding form, the fabrication of an entire alimentary order — provisioning giving way to cultivation, cultivation to domestication — until the species feeds itself wholly from within an artificial ecosystem? The answer follows from the character of the original lack. The misfit was, at root, alimentary: an ape under nutritional pressure in a world that no longer fed it as it had fed its forest-dwelling ancestors. Obligatory artifice, directed from the first flake toward the problem of food, naturally elaborates along the alimentary axis before any other. The artificial food chain is the human's native object precisely because the founding misfit was a hunger — and it is the construction of that chain, more than any other single achievement, that lifts the human clear of the web of life and converts its AOS into a world.
## B. Naming the made: why "artificiality," not "technology"
 If the made is to carry the weight this argument places on it, it needs a name — and the obvious candidate, technology, will not serve, for reasons worth stating carefully rather than by fiat. In its now-dominant usage, technology names a means to an end: a neutral instrument, indifferent to the purposes it serves, awaiting good or bad use by its wielder. The Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy registers the common view in just these terms — technology as "a neutral instrument" that is good or bad only in "the use to which it is put," or as "merely applied science." The conception descends from Heidegger's diagnosis of the "current" understanding, which he summarizes as the twofold commonplace that technology is "a means to an end" and "a human activity" (Heidegger, 1977: 4–5) — the "instrumental and anthropological" definition he sets out to question (Heidegger, 1977: 4–5).
 The trouble is that this means-end framing, smuggled in with the word, distorts most of what I need the name to cover. Call religion a "technology" and one has already presumed it to be a device for producing some effect — when religion, on its own self-understanding, is a matter of revelation or devotion, not an instrument calibrated to an end. Call science a "technology" and one imports the same presumption — when science, whatever its applications, is in the first instance a truth-seeking practice answerable to the world, not a tool answerable to a user. To be sure, there are influential inversions of the neutral-instrument picture: Foucault's "technologies of the self," which treat ethical self-formation as a kind of craft, and Yuk Hui's "cosmotechnics," which embeds technical activity within a moral and cosmological order. These are defensible, even illuminating, redescriptions — but they earn their force precisely by straining against the word's ordinary means-end sense, and I do not want a term whose every use must first fight its own connotations.
 Stiegler makes the same complaint from the other side. In ordinary use, he observes, "in general, technics designates… [the] restricted and specified domain of tools, of instruments, if not only machines" — an understanding he calls "inadequate," "already restrictive and derived" (Stiegler, 1998: 93–94). Against this narrowing he restores the older, wider sense: "Technics (tekhne) designates… all the domains of skill," so that "all human action… is after a fashion tekhne" (Stiegler, 1998: 93). It is this breadth I require — but rather than reclaim the contested word technics against its modern drift, I will adopt a term that wears the breadth on its face.
 That term is artificiality. By it I name not a domain of objects but an origin and a mode of being: the condition of being humanly made, instituted, and maintained, as against being given. Artificiality, so defined, does not pick out the tools-and-machines sense of "technology" at all; it picks out everything the human produces to secure its life — the field and the city, the law and the rite, the word and the institution, the science and the creed. On this usage, science, ethics, and religion are not technologies (they are not instruments calibrated to ends) but kinds of artificiality (they are humanly made and maintained). The word is chosen with its root in view: artificial descends from the Latin ars, skill or craft, and facere, to make, so that the artificial is, etymologically, simply that which is brought about by human making, as against that which arises of itself.
 Two independent traditions converge on just this breadth, and the convergence is what licenses treating it as a natural category rather than a stipulation. Stiegler's wide technē, already cited, "designates first and foremost all the domains of skill" — he names "politeness," "cooking," "rhetoric," "poetry," finding "something of poetry and rhetoric in all language" (Stiegler, 1998: 93). From an entirely different direction, Obara marks the same field in Japanese orthography, writing the made as モノ [ADLER: always lead with romanization/transliteration or translation of Japanese or Chinese, with its original phrase/character in parentheses. I'll only repeat this once in the comments, unless the romanization is unique] in katakana — distinguishing it from the everyday もの of ordinary things — to gather everything humanly produced, the abstract no less than the concrete: religion, ideology, and music alongside the axe and the granary. Two thinkers working in different languages and traditions, reaching independently for a category this wide, suggest the category is found rather than imposed.
 To see that the boundary between given and made is itself drawn differently by different civilizations — and so that no single drawing can be taken for granted — it helps to set the Western vocabulary beside the Chinese. The most instructive term is Xunzi's 偽. In the "Human Nature Is Bad" chapter he writes, "人之性惡，其善者偽也" [ADLER: always lead with romanization/transliteration or translation of Japanese or Chinese, with its original phrase/character in parentheses -- the only once repeated here]— human nature is foul (惡), and what is good in it is artifice (偽) — and glosses the term: "可學而能、可事而成之在人者謂之偽," that in a person which can be learned and brought to completion through effort is called 偽. The renderings are Paul Goldin's: 惡 not as "evil" in any Augustinian sense but as the foul — the raw, undisciplined impulse — and 偽 as artifice; and 性 (xing) names not a moral essence but the inborn, the biological given one is simply born with, an etymon Goldin traces to "genus, stock" and to 生 (sheng), "birth, life" (Goldin, 2026). The Tang commentator Yang Liang spells out the graph itself: "偽字人傍為" — the character 偽 is 人 (person) beside 為 (doing): the made is, literally, the person-done. And the etymon of 偽 is telling — it carries the root sense "to forge" (Goldin, 2026), the very sense this thesis will later give the metaverse's 偽道, the forged Dao. The tradition thus names the made not by opposition to a reified "nature" but as the artifice human effort lays over a raw biological given, the human completing what the given leaves unfinished.
 There is an irony in both vocabularies worth pausing on, because it bears directly on my choice of term. The Latin artificialis curdled, over time, toward the pejorative — "artificial" came to mean fake, contrived, insincere. The Chinese 偽 curdled the same way: the Shuowen jiezi defines it flatly as "偽，詐也。从人爲聲" — 偽 means deceit; it takes 人 as sense and 爲 as sound. Both traditions, that is, let the word for the made slide into the word for the false — as though to make were already to falsify. Recovering 偽 in Xunzi's neutral, even honorific sense is exactly the operation this thesis performs on "artificial": reclaiming, for the made, a name that ordinary usage has soured.
 One caution must be entered before going further, lest the comparison claim more than it should. I take from Xunzi a name and a structural insight — that a civilization can mark the made without opposing it to a reified nature — not his substantive doctrine. Xunzi himself does not collapse the 性/偽 distinction [ADLER: the point should be Xunzi doesn't follow Nature/Man(culture) divide]; he insists on it, writing in the "Discourse on Ritual" that "性者，本始材朴也；偽者，文理隆盛也" — nature is the raw material of the beginning; 偽 is the flourishing of pattern and order — and in the "Rectifying Names" chapter that "心慮而能為之動謂之偽," the deliberate activity arising when the mind reflects and acts is called 偽. (English renderings of 偽 vary tellingly — Hutton gives "deliberate effort," Knoblock "conscious exertion," Goldin "artifice"; I follow Goldin's renderings — "artifice" for 偽, "foul" for 惡 — for the reasons given above.) The point of the cross-traditional detour is not to enlist Xunzi as an ally in doctrine but to show, by counter-example, that the Western partition of given from made is one contingent settlement among possible others — and therefore that adopting "artificiality" as a term is itself an artificial act, to be performed deliberately rather than inherited unexamined.
 The Greek vocabulary that the modern term inherits drew the line where the Chinese did not, and the seam runs along the contrast between the grown and the made. Phusis — "nature" — derives from the Indo-European root *bʰeh₂u-, "to grow, bring forth"; in Aristotle's canonical formulation (Physics II.1, 192b8) what is by phusis has the principle of its motion within itself. Against it stands technē — "craft, art" — from *teks-, "to fabricate," the root that surfaces in τέκτων, the carpenter or builder: the made as what has the principle of its coming-to-be in another, namely its maker. The Greeks add a third, nomos — "law, custom, convention" — from νέμω, "to distribute, apportion" (Indo-European *nem-): what holds not by nature but by human positing. (These Indo-European reconstructions follow Watkins, 2011; for φύω in laryngeal notation cf. Beekes, 2010 — the full grade *bʰeh₂u- given here is a variant of the root also cited in its zero grade *bʰuh₂-.)
 Now the comparative point can be made precisely, and it is subtler than a simple presence-or-absence of concepts. Yuk Hui states the asymmetry directly: "the Chinese have no equivalents of the categories that the Greeks called techne and physis" (Hui, 2016: 33–34). But the right inference is not that Chinese thought lacked a concept of the made; it is that it grouped the field differently. Rémi Brague helps locate the Greek grouping. What the Greeks set on one side, he observes, is "that which has its principle in the human activity of fabrication or estimation, i.e., the artificial (tekhnē) and the conventional (nomos)," and on the other "that which grows by itself… the natural" (Brague, 2003: 14). The decisive move here is the grouping, not an identity: technē and nomos are bracketed together — the fabricated and the agreed, the made object and the made norm — and jointly opposed to phusis, the self-arising. The Western partition is thus two-against-one: the made and the conventional on one side, the grown on the other. Chinese thought, organizing the field around 道 and 器 — the way and the vessel, the ordering principle and the particular instrument, the 器 (qi) being the made-as-instrument situated within a cosmic-moral order rather than set over against nature — simply does not run the same seam [ADLER: this is an example of world-universe embeddedness which is the default of most civilizations and will be elaborated in the coming chapters]. I dwell on this not to adjudicate which carving is correct but to expose the contingency: in choosing "artificiality," I deliberately take the broad bracket — technē-and-nomos together, the whole field of the humanly made and instituted — as my object, rather than the narrow, machine-bound "technology" that the same tradition's later drift bequeathed.
 
## C. Why the made is irreducible to the [ADLER: replace "given" with "material"]: virtuality [ADLER: "as causally efficacious nonmaterial artificiality", remove "and its two registers] [ADLER: this section is overall terrible and require FACTCHECK & RECONSTRUCTION -- tell me how would you reconstruct pre or during the humanization stage. you must also evaluate why your factchecking missed this and do not make the same mistake again. some scaffolds with verbatim excerpts should be available in 30drafts. only reconstruct after I approve your plan]
[ADLER: integrate my original draft: ```While it might be easy to picture how tangible material technologies have extensively reshaped our worlds from cave- and stone-based tribes to AI-powered “smart cities,” artificiality is not always manifested in material forms. The nonmaterial forms of artificiality are indispensable for the development of material counterparts and dialectically lead to each other. Artificially created nonmaterial things, such as gods, money, and laws, have the power to direct our thoughts and behaviors or even elicit emotions or biological reflexes (e.g., fight or flight responses). Without these nonmaterial things or aspects that facilitate the interactions or dynamics among its components, a world as “an artificial environment nested in the universe for human survival” will not be possible, as material things will just be aggregates without being integrated into functioning systems.```]
 A breadth this great invites an obvious objection, and meeting it sharpens the central claim rather than merely defending it. If artificiality takes in science along with the plough, does the category not swallow everything, collapsing the very distinction between the made and the true that inquiry depends on? Science, after all, tracks a world it did not invent; surely it cannot be "made" in the same sense as a statute or a coin. The objection has force only if one assumes that to be made is to be false — and that assumption is exactly what Chapter I's rejection of the nature/culture dualism removes. That a thing is built carries no implication that it fails to track what is there: the eye is built by evolution, and this is no argument against its seeing. Scientific knowledge is artificial in my sense — assembled by method, instrument, and institution — and at the same time answerable to the given, which is precisely why it can be done well or badly and why its claims can be wrong. To call science artificial is not to demote it to fiction; it is to locate it, correctly, as a human work about a world that exceeds the work. The universe remains the substrate; the made is the structure raised upon it; and structures can be both made and faithful. [ADLER: perhaps move this to the section above? Alternatively, since both are instances of virtuality, it can be split into two: one goes to the previous section, one can be kept here, but their link to virtuality must be made explicit.] 
 With the objection cleared, the central claim of the chapter can be stated without hedging. The world the human builds is irreducible to the universe that underlies it. I do not mean that the world floats free of matter — every artefact is some arrangement of matter and obeys matter's laws. I mean that what an artefact is, and what it can do, cannot be read off from the matter that composes it: a made thing has properties and exercises powers that its material substrate neither possesses nor explains, and a world built of such things is therefore a stratum of reality with laws of its own, not derivable from the physics beneath it. The argument turns on identifying the precise feature of the made that the universe cannot supply.
 That feature is what I will call virtuality. The made works, very often, through something that is causally efficacious yet nowhere material — a status, a rule, a meaning, a relation that does real work in the world without being any of the matter through which it works. I call this causally efficacious nonmaterial aspect of artificiality virtuality. Two qualifications fix the term against predictable misreadings. First, virtuality is an aspect of artificiality, not a separate category of thing: every virtuality is an artificiality, but not every artificiality is virtual — a wall compels by its sheer mass, not by any meaning [ADLER: although the meaning side of it also has causal efficacy], and is artificial without being, in the relevant sense, virtual [ADLER: what do you mean by "... without being"?]. Second, and against the word's everyday connotations, virtuality is not unreality. It is the opposite: the dimension in which the made does its most consequential work. The authority in an office, the obligation in a promise, the value in money, the boundary in a border — each is fully effective and nowhere locatable in the matter that bears it, material only in the way a melody is "in" the air that carries it, which is to say not reducible to that air at all.
 Because "virtual" is a worked-over word, I should mark my sense off from three established conceptions, keeping from each what is sound and declining what would mislead. The first is the older philosophical sense preserved by Charles Peirce, for whom a virtual X is "something, not an X, which has the efficiency (virtus) of an X" (Peirce, 1902). From Peirce I keep the root: virtus, efficacy — virtuality is defined by what it does. But I decline the "not an X" clause, which makes the virtual a substitute for some real thing it is not; in my sense the money in the banknote is not a stand-in for some realer money elsewhere — it is money, fully and without remainder. [ADLER: this still differs without justifying the difference. cf. original draft: ```This is different from the popular view of the term, which sees a virtual thing as a simulated replica of something real, meaning that it possesses certain characteristics or features of the simulated real thing yet is not completely real. This simulationist view can be traced back to C. S. Pierce’s definition (1902): “a virtual x (where x is a common noun) is something, not an x, which has the efficiency (virtus) of an x” (Emphasis added).
While my usage of virtuality carries on the “efficiency (virtus)” aspect in Pierce’s definition, which comes from its Latin etymon virtus (i.e., virtue) and virtualis (i.e., power or potency),   I believe the “as if” aspect in his definition is unhelpful. As Jean Baudrillard shows (1994), many artificial things have no original or real counterparts to represent whatsoever. Seeing a virtual x as a simulation of the real x overlooks the fact that many virtual things are novel, and a virtual x may be original in its own right. Without a radical departure from this view, we cannot make sense of an emerging future where we are increasingly incapable of finding the real represented or simulated by the virtual.```] [AI scaffold in Ch-2-6_v4_non-computational-virtuality_prose: ```These lines carry forward the *virtus* (power/efficacy) sense from Peirce's etymology while rejecting the "as if" component on Baudrillard's ground that many virtual things have no original to which they refer. A virtual *x* can be original in its own right — a point that matters most for an emerging stage in which the real-represented may no longer be locatable.```]
 The second is Jean Baudrillard's, whose analysis of simulation — the copy without an original, the sign that no longer dissimulates a reality but precedes it — I draw on with precision and with a clear limit (Baudrillard, 1994). I keep his sharp distinction between dissimulating (masking a reality that is there) and simulating (generating a "reality" with no original behind it). But I decline the terminal, epochal framing in which [ADLER: the "virtual", not "simulation"] names a late historical condition — an "integral reality" that has finally displaced the real. Virtuality, in my account, is not a stage that modernity reaches; it is a trans-historical genus, as old as money and law and rite, of which the digital is only the latest and most concentrated species. [AI scaffold in Ch-2-6_v4_non-computational-virtuality_prose: ```A reader fluent in Baudrillard's later writings will recognise that his usage of "virtuality" is more elaborate than the Phase-1 ally-against-Peirce use established in ¶3a. From *Passwords*, *The Impossible Exchange*, and *The Intelligence of Evil*, Baudrillard treats *the Virtual* as a terminal-eschatological category — the "final solution of the real," in which not only the real but the sign itself is annihilated and "Integral Reality" replaces the symbolic order. The thesis declines this framing on two grounds. First, Baudrillard organises his account of virtuality around a *historical-epochal carving* (symbolic → real → hyperreal → virtual) in which the virtual is a terminal stage exclusive to the digital-algorithmic phase; the thesis treats virtuality as a *trans-historical genus* present from oral ritual onward, of which the algorithmic stage is one substrate-instance and not the genus's terminal negation. Second, what Baudrillard calls "the symbolic" (gift, sacrifice, ritual obligation, reversibility — the pre-value relational order) is, on the thesis's account, a *part of* virtuality (the institutional-fact / customary-norm zone), whereas Baudrillard treats it as virtuality's opposite.  The third is David Chalmers's, the most demanding because it is the closest to a rival. This is virtuality in the sense the thesis adopts. The thesis and Chalmers therefore agree about a great deal (the reality of virtual money; the substrate-neutrality of information; the inadequacy of fictionalism about virtual entities) and are doing different jobs with the word "virtuality" — Chalmers's job is the *defence of virtual entities against fictionalism*; the thesis's job is the *characterisation of virtuality as a constitutive layer of human worlds and the diagnosis of its operational transformation* under the metaverse-stage substrate-shift (developed in §IV).```]
 [ADLER: footnote in original draft, perhaps worth diving deeper and promoting and integrating into main text, to highlight what Chalmers overlooks? decide where and how to integrate during reconstruction ```The idea of virtuality in this thesis is in alignment with that of sociologist Anthony Giddens and philosopher Roy Bhaskar. According to Giddens (1984), social structures exist virtually – Here, virtuality is certainly not a “unreal or not-as-real representation or simulation” of something else that is truly “real,” but it refers to the idea that social structures are human-dependent and do not exist materially per se. Bhaskar also considers the virtual not as something “contrasting reality, but as constituting a sui generis real emergent stratum of reality with its own irreducible causal powers” (Engholm, 2015).```]
 Money is the cleanest case, and Searle states it definitively. "It is only given the institution of money," he writes, "that I now have a five dollar bill in my hand. Take away the institution and all I have is a piece of paper with various gray and green markings" (Searle, 1969: 51). The constitutive form of the fact is "such and such counts as a $20 bill in our society" (Searle, 2008a: 33); and where the money is electronic the point is starker still, for "there need be no physical reality to the $1,000, other than the representation" (Searle, 2008b: 457). The monetary fact is not in the paper, and where there is no paper there is no less money. The banknote is thus the exemplary irreducible: wholly dependent on a material substrate, and yet, in everything that makes it money, not reducible to that substrate at all. This is the social-ontology tradition's central insight (Searle, 1995), and it sits comfortably within the stratified, emergent-powers realism this thesis took from Bhaskar (2008) in Chapter I: a higher stratum, dependent on the lower, with causal powers of its own that the lower does not possess.
 
 [ADLER: i want to keep a prose like this in my original draft, that illustrates how the causally efficacious nonmaterial artificiality has always been integral to the world, despite its forms and substrates change over time: ```Initially, humans told stories of gods and practiced ceremonies with the hope of bettering their lives in the vastly unknown and unpredictable universe. These orally communicated gods and embodied ceremonies formed our worlds’ nonmaterial yet causally efficacious basis. Over time, in many places, gods have been replaced by science(s) and ceremonies by laws, which are documented and conveyed by writing. Nowadays, we are increasingly receiving guidance from AI and regulated by algorithms through computer-based interfaces and objects.```]That virtuality is trans-historical, not a digital novelty, has been argued before: Massimo Durante notes that the human "has always dealt with the virtual, ever since it lived by stories collectively invented" (Durante, 2022) [ADLER: LOOKUP vallorOxfordHandbookPhilosophy2022 to see if there are any resources from Durante to argue against Chalmers or at least justify my different definition]. Viewing virtuality as causally efficacious nonmaterial artificiality also helps us navigate the ever-blurring boundaries between computer-based and non-computer-based reality. As Brey notices importantly (2003), while "rocks and trees" or "screwdrivers and chairs" can only be simulated, "money can literally exist" in a digital or computer-based environment. Although money is increasingly losing its materiality as the digital has become the default mode of existence in our time, we do not call the nonmaterial money in our banks "virtual money." Money is still money, and money has always been virtual. Shells, grain, salt, metal, paper, or digits are merely temporary media to operationalize money's virtuality. Brey does not notice that the reason why technological objects such as screwdrivers and chairs can only be simulated but not reproduced in a digital environment may not be due to the functional limitations of such an environment, as he thinks. Given that artificiality is the exteriorization of our organs and memories to perform certain functions to fulfill our needs, those objects are not reproducible because they are redundant or simply "use-less" — the conditions for such specific modes of functioning or usage are absent in such systems. A screwdriver is use-less since "tightening up something" in a digital environment requires an algorithm rather than a physical object that "screws"; a chair is also use-less since there is neither gravity nor the physical soreness of our legs that desires a physical object to rest upon. Simulating a screwdriver or a chair in a digital environment may only be a function of attending to our perceptual inertia or, someday, nostalgia. This is evidenced by the fact that while people in the past used scissors and glue to cut and paste two-dimensional objects such as text and pictures from one place to another, we now simply cut and paste in our digital devices without the hassle of simulating scissors and glue — later generations might not even know what scissors and glue are, if all cutting-and-pasting activities, including art creation, become digitized. The use-lessness of many material objects in digital environments also exemplifies another key feature of artificial environments. While artificial environments are dependent on and constrained by the materiality and laws of the universe, they are not reducible to the latter (Polanyi's boundary condition, introduced in Chapter I): they have organizing principles and conditions of their own. In purely biological terms, the physically fittest Homo has the best odds of surviving in the wilderness, yet in a world — an artificial environment — this may no longer be the case: they might have a higher mortality rate as they are designated to the role of hunting, war, or heavy labor, while a physically unfit person might utilize their charisma or wit to concentrate power and resources, which in turn benefits their survival. As a re-emphasis of the AOS criteria: despite all humans being subject to the materiality and laws of the universe, the local materiality and laws of the artificial environments — namely, the mode of artificiality, or worlds — that direct their members' adaptation or domesticate them are not reducible to the former.
 Searle's framework lets us say what kind of fact this is, and also where I must part from him. He distinguishes brute facts, which obtain independently of human institutions (the mass of a stone), from institutional facts, which obtain only within them (the value of a coin); [ADLER: social facts should be introduced first, as it is the genus for institutional facts? VERIFY] institutional facts are erected by status functions of the form "X counts as Y in context C," and they carry deontic powers — rights, duties, entitlements, authorizations — that move people as surely as any physical force (Searle, 1995). My virtuality overlaps this notion but is wider than it, and the difference is deliberate. Searle gates institutional reality behind language and collective acceptance, which makes it constitutively human. I draw the line elsewhere — at the causally efficacious nonmaterial as such, whether or not it is language-gated — so that every Searlean institutional fact is a virtuality, but not every virtuality need be a Searlean institutional fact. The reason for drawing it more widely appears the moment we look below the human line.
 For virtuality has an evolutionary floor, and the most economical way to show it is Obara's. Consider the gorilla's chest-drumming. Obara describes it as "ゴリラが真の攻撃に入る前の儀式として、胸叩き（ドラミング）をしている" — a ritual the gorilla performs before entering into true combat — one that "戦いを避ける機能を果たす," serves the function of avoiding a fight (Obara, pp. 17–19). The drumming does real causal work — it establishes deference, orders the troop, forestalls violence — and it does so by what it means, not by what it physically delivers; no blow is struck, yet the outcome is settled. The same holds for the courtship displays Obara catalogues among cranes and ibex, where a patterned performance "所期の目的を果たす," accomplishes its intended end, in the reproductive register (Obara, pp. 22–26). These displays are not institutional facts in Searle's sense — they lack the linguistic, conventional, and deontic articulation that institutional facts require, which is why Searle places them outside institutional reality. But it would be wrong to say he excludes them altogether: they are, in his own terms, social facts — cases of collective intentionality, which he holds require "no cultural apparatus, cultural conventions, or language," his own instance being a pack of hyenas hunting a lion, which "and Congress passing legislation are both cases of social facts," the institutional being merely "a special subclass" of the social (Searle, 1995: 37–38). My quarrel with Searle is therefore narrower, and falls at the social/institutional boundary rather than the human/animal one: where he gates the rich apparatus of status and deontic power behind language, I draw the line at causal efficacy by meaning as such, so that the prelinguistic social fact already counts as virtuality. This is why I call these displays proto-virtual: causally efficacious by meaning rather than mass, social in Searle's full sense, but not yet woven into a fabric of stated rules. [ADLER: not sure if the quarrel is relevant to my definition of artificiality, since Searle himself doesn't use this term? VERIFY]
 That the line between the merely social and the properly institutional should fall exactly at language — and so exactly at the human — is itself a substantive commitment, not a neutral observation, and one that animal-cognition research and posthumanist thought increasingly press. Eduardo Viveiros de Castro's Amerindian perspectivism furnishes the sharpest counter-case, and pointedly against Searle by name: in the perspectival ontology "what we see as 'nature' is seen by other species as 'culture,' i.e., as institutional facts — what we see as blood, a natural substance, is seen by jaguars as manioc beer, an artefact," so that "these institutional facts are here universal, something that is quite foreign to Searle's alternatives" (Viveiros de Castro, 2015: 255; the canonical formulation, "what to us is blood, is maize beer to the jaguar," is at 1998: 478). One need not adopt perspectivism wholesale to take its methodological point: the confinement of the institutional, and of deontic standing, to the linguistic human is a contestable boundary — and the proto-virtual marks where the question is forced rather than where it is settled. Their existence makes the decisive point. Virtuality is not what computation added to the world; it is something the world has run on since before there were humans to formalize it.
 
 [ADLER: the following should be in a separate section, since the typology applies to both nonmaterial and material]
 [ADLER: remove: "Before assembling these threads into a map of the made, one more distinction is needed, because virtuality does not always bind in the same way." reopen this new section.] Artificiality compels in two registers, and the difference between them will organize the rest of this thesis. In its conventional register, the made holds the way nomos holds: it compels because, and to the extent that, humans keep enacting it — and keep conceiving it as binding. These are the two dependencies Bhaskar identified in social structures: their activity-dependence [ADLER: remove: (which I will call enactment-dependence)] and their concept-dependence (Bhaskar, 1998). A statute governs through the standing apparatus that enforces it — police, courts, prisons; a social norm governs through sanction; a commandment governs through belief. Let the enacting practice lapse — let belief fail, enforcement cease, the sanction go unapplied — and the artificiality lapses with it, for it was never anything but sustained. In its structural register, by contrast, the made compels not because anyone keeps accepting it but because it has been built into the very arrangement of the environment, so that it conditions action whether or not it is acknowledged. A locked door needs no enforcer; an algorithm sorts regardless of whether anyone believes in it; a paywall forecloses a page without convening a court. Here violation is not deterred but foreclosed — removed from the space of available actions rather than punished after the fact.
 The structural register must be characterized with care, because the temptation is to say the made has here "become nature," and that is not quite right. The structural is not bare phusis — there is nothing self-arising about a paywall or a dam; every such thing is engineered, and a designer stands behind it. It is, more exactly, phusis-in-operation: a made thing whose origin is wholly artificial but whose operation has the as-though-given character of a natural constraint, confronting the agent with the brute non-negotiability of a cliff while remaining, in its provenance, entirely a human work [ADLER: too anthropocentric]. When in later chapters I describe modernity's drift as a technē coming to stand as a phusis, this is what I mean — not that the made literally acquires nature's principle of self-arising, but that it migrates from the conventional register into the structural, coming to operate with the unanswerable authority of the given while never ceasing to be made. That migration, reserved for full treatment in the chapters on the metaverse, is the engine of the squeeze.
 Two clarifications keep this distinction from hardening into something it is not. First, the two registers name a dominant tendency, not an absolute partition: most artificialities mix them. [ADLER: remove "A legal order is largely conventional, yet it leans on structural supports — the built apparatus of enforcement, the registries and barriers that make its rulings bite" and replace with railway system as example: the material rails, electricity circuits, etc = structural; how the railway is designed, operative protocols, etc = conventional]; a platform is largely structural, yet its architecture is set by policies that remain, in principle, revisable. The squeeze, as later chapters will argue, is in part the progressive shrinking of the conventional margin — the space of what Habermas calls communicative reason [ADLER: "is possible" but "not necessarily exercised"], where the made holds only by ongoing agreement — as a system matures: the steady conversion of what was negotiable into what is merely operative (a colonization I return to, with Habermas, in Chapter IV). Second, the distinction lets me make precise an old contrast between rule and law. A rule or norm is conventional: its violation is punished from outside, by sanction, and remains always possible to commit — etiquette breached, a customary observance ignored. A law, in the structural sense I am giving the word, is one whose violation is prevented from within, foreclosed by the arrangement itself — the locked door [ADLER: this is actual, structural, but not law; the law is the real beneath it -- physics of friction and rigidity, etc.], the code that simply will not execute the forbidden transaction. This is why it is no mere metaphor to say the metaverse has "laws of its own": it exhibits a structural homology with the laws of the universe, the two differing not in their compelling character but only in their source — the one naturally given, the other humanly engineered. A law in this fullest sense is not a mere structural arrangement at the level of the actual but an organizing principle at the level of the real — which is why the metaverse's "laws of its own," the 偽道 [ADLER: Chinese only again? -- analyze why this is not corrected in earlier rounds] of later chapters, sit at the deepest stratum and not among its surface structures.
 It bears repeating that this structural compulsion is not yet present in the proto-virtual displays with which animals furnish the evolutionary floor. The gorilla's drumming and the crane's courtship compel by meaning, but conventionally and locally, sustained by enactment [ADLER: perhaps too anthropomorphic? but this possibility cannot be ruled out. actually, i think their cases show the ambiguous continuum between the conventional and structural -- the Enlightenment view (like Bacon or Descartes) would view these as mechanistic, biologically determined behaviors "without free will", but now we know this is to anthropocentric; to view these as compelled by meaning? although might be too anthropomorphic, but being somewhere near the voluntary end, makes Chalmers "computer-based view" feel somewhat off -- although Chalmers doesn't reject the simulation theory and believe it's totally possible that we're currently living a live in a simulation]; they do not foreclose. Nor do the documented animal "traditions" amount yet to a world. Whiten and colleagues describe "cultural differences (often known as traditions in ethology)… maintained through… social transmission" — the "dialects of songbirds," the "sweet-potato washing of Japanese macaques" (Whiten et al., 1999: 682). These are the raw material of a made world — proto-virtual meaning, socially transmitted — but not yet the thing itself, because they neither ratchet into an open-ended order nor harden into the structural register where the made operates with the authority of the given. [ADLER: not sure why the "not yet a world" needs to be placed here. seems irrelevant to the typology]
 We are now in a position to map the made as a whole, and the map repays the effort because it shows not merely that the world is irreducible to the universe but how the irreducibility varies. Three distinctions, assembled, generate the map. The first two are the ones just drawn: the register in which an artificiality compels (conventional or structural) and the manner in which it works (through material arrangement or through virtuality). The third is the two-depths distinction Chapter I drew within the world itself — between its enduring conditions and principles (the deeper layer, which in the critical-realist idiom corresponds to the domain of the real, and which the Chinese tradition would mark as 道 ) and its particular structures and dynamics (the layer of the actual, which the same tradition would mark as 理 ). Crossing register against aspect, once at the level of particular structures and once at the level of enduring conditions, yields two tables. They make visible a single thesis: the made is irreducible to the given everywhere, but the irreducibility is thicker in some configurations than in others, and the gradient is itself diagnostic. [ADLER: CJK problems again, and they're not needed here, unless you justify]
Table 2-1. Configurations of the made at the level of particular structures and dynamics (the actual / 理).

| | material | nonmaterial (virtual) |
|---|---|---|
| structural | A wall; a door — forecloses by built shape. | A paywall; a DRM lock — forecloses by authored code. |
| conventional | A coin; a stamped passport — bears a conferred status. | A statute; a contract — a pure made-meaning, sustained by enactment. |

 Read across and down, Table 2-1 elaborates as follows. The structural-material cell holds the wall and the door: a made physical form that forecloses or affords by its sheer shape, needing no enforcer and no belief, but irreducible to the universe only by its arrangement — for the universe already forecloses by mass and by gravity, which is why this is the thinnest irreducibility of the four. The structural-virtual cell holds the paywall and the digital-rights lock: an authored rule built directly into operation, foreclosing a page or a file by code rather than by mass, and so combining authored pattern with operational foreclosure — made like a meaning, yet binding like a wall. Beneath them, the conventional-material cell holds the coin and the stamped passport: a material object whose force lies not in its metal or paper but in a conferred status that no physical examination of the object would ever disclose. And the conventional-virtual cell holds the statute and the contract: a pure made-meaning, doubly non-material, since neither its substrate nor its binding force is physical, sustained in being by nothing but collective enactment. Across the row, then, the made grows harder as it moves from convention to structure; down the column, it grows more purely non-material as it moves from material to virtual.
Table 2-2. Configurations of the made at the level of enduring conditions and principles (the real / 道). [ADLER: this is outdated. revise according to Ch-2-C_typology-v5-UPDATE-stratified-organized-organizing_2026-06-06 -- and our conversation history, since the 0606 note is partial. ask me questions to clarify whenever uncertain. the following descriptions should also be updated after your revision]

| | material | nonmaterial (virtual) |
|---|---|---|
| structural | The built environment; infrastructure — a made arrangement scaled into terrain. | Code-as-law; the self-completing order (自足完成化) — an authored architecture that runs itself. |
| conventional | (no stable régime-level case — collapses into the cells below and right) | Money; property; the rule of law — a foundational order of conferred statuses. |

 Table 2-2 runs the same two axes at the deeper level — that of enduring conditions and standing orders rather than particular artefacts — and the cells shift accordingly. The structural-material cell now holds the built environment and infrastructure: not a single wall but a made material arrangement scaled into a standing régime, a whole landscape of roads, grids, and buildings that shapes all movement within it and, over generations, comes to be encountered as terrain. This is the chief site where a technē, through sheer endurance, comes to stand as a phusis. The structural-virtual cell holds code-as-law and the self-completing order (自足完成化): an authored architecture that runs as a self-operating whole, fusing maximal made-ness with maximal foreclosure — the deepest cell of the grid, and the one I will identify in later chapters as the metaverse's genuine 偽道, a made way that operates with the unanswerable authority of the given. The conventional-virtual cell holds money, property, and the rule of law: a foundational order of conferred statuses, the thickest purely made order of meaning, on which an entire economy or polity rests without any physical substrate of its own. The remaining cell — conventional-material at régime scale — has no stable occupant: a standing order resting on bare material convention does not hold as a distinct type but collapses into the conventional-virtual order beside it and into the artefact-level cases of Table 2-1. The empty cell is itself instructive: at the level of enduring conditions, the material and the conventional do not combine into anything that lasts.
 Reading the grid rewards attention, because the irreducibility it charts is not uniform but graded, and it grades along two axes that pull in opposite directions. Move from the conventional toward the structural and, in one respect, the made comes to resemble the given more closely: a convention requires a mind to keep accepting it, which is something the universe does not contain, whereas a structural constraint requires only a made form, and the universe is full of forms that foreclose — the cliff, the gravitational well. Move instead from the virtual toward the material and the made again leans toward the given: a virtuality has no physical substrate at all, while a material artificiality rides on one, borrowing the universe's own matter to do its work. The thinnest irreducibility therefore sits in the structural-material cell — a made form imposed on the universe's matter, running on the universe's physics — and this is exactly where, in the chapters to come, I will locate the drift of a technē into a phusis: enduring infrastructure that hardens, over generations, into something indistinguishable from terrain. The thickest irreducibility sits at the opposite corner, in the conventional-virtual cell — a pure order of made meaning, belief-dependent and substrate-free, that the universe could never produce or sustain on its own.
 It is the remaining corner, however, that the whole thesis is built to reach. The structural-virtual cell combines the two maxima at once: maximal binding — it forecloses, like a wall — and maximal made-ness — it is an authored rule with no physical substrate, binding like a meaning. An artificiality of this kind forecloses action with the unanswerable authority of nature while remaining wholly, visibly a human work; it is as hard as a cliff and as made as a promise. This is why the squeeze, when it arrives at its limit, is uniquely tight. The conventional-virtual order, however foundational, can in principle be renegotiated, because it lives by acceptance; the structural-material order, however hard, at least wears its physical constraints openly. But a structural-virtual order presses with the necessity of the given and the deniability of the made, foreclosing like nature while never even looking natural enough to be mistaken for it — and so leaving its subject neither room to renegotiate nor ground to stand and refuse. The metaverse, I will argue, is the configuration that occupies this corner fully; the rest of the thesis is an account of what it is to live squeezed inside it.
## D. The autonomy of the made, the gap it opens, and its double edge [ADLER: This entire section is too crude. I want you to revisit your earlier simulations and our discussions and scaffold drafts, in particular this one "Ch-2-D_double-edgedness-of-artificiality", to come up with a new structure and only rewrite after approval.]
 Two properties of this irreducible artificiality remain to be drawn out, and together they carry the chapter into the rest of the thesis. The first is autonomy. Because the made has [ADLER: replace "laws" with "organizing conditions and principles"] of its own — this was the burden of the previous section — it acquires, in the aggregate, a momentum of its own. A tool, once made, has [ADLER: ~~a logic~~ too strong. replace with "affordances and constrants" as the structure (actual) made possible by its material properties as organizing conditions (real)"] of its own; [ADLER: "once tools are being used, their affordances, contraints, and material properties"] beget tools, [ADLER: ~~by the doubling of working~~]; and the world of tools, once underway, develops by a directionality no single maker chooses, soliciting the next tool and the next [ADLER: too strong and abstract, needs better framing based on complexity/self-organization/recursivity in plain language without jargon]. The second property follows from the first: this self-amplifying made-world turns back upon its maker and remakes him. The human that built the artificial environment began, within it, to be bred by it — recall that the brain was finished by the world it had started to build — and so there opened a gap between the biological animal the human remains and the accelerating artifice it now inhabits. Obara names that gap a ズレ (zure), a dis-synchrony: "the change in the systems and the things produced, and the change in the biological human, differ in speed and in kind, and a zure arises." That gap, appearing first at the scale of the single mismatched body, is the earliest form of the structure this thesis calls the squeeze.
 The second property is that autonomy has an edge, and the edge cuts both ways. Because the made runs ahead of its maker, artificiality is double-edged — and this is no peculiarity of the digital age. Ivan Illich gave the dynamic its classic historical statement. Every industrial institution, he argued, crosses two watersheds: at the first it achieves a genuine good, and at the second the good inverts, the institution coming to defeat the very purpose it was built to serve. Past the second watershed it enacts what Illich called a radical monopoly — not one firm's dominance over a market, but the elimination of the very conditions under which alternatives could exist. The automobile remakes a city into its own image until walking is no longer a real option; the curtailment of that option, foreclosed by the rebuilt environment, is the radical monopoly.
 Stiegler names the same ambivalence with a term I will carry forward: artificiality is pharmacological. The Greek pharmakon is at once remedy and poison, and every exteriorized artificiality, once it has become independent of its user, can return as toxic — writing supplements memory and, Plato feared, atrophies it; the platforms that extend our sociality also industrialize it. The crucial feature of the pharmakon is that it is undetermined: which edge dominates — cure or poison — is not fixed in advance by the artificiality itself but settled by what Stiegler calls a regime of care, the ongoing counter-work by which a made thing is kept to its better edge. Autonomy is precisely what makes that counter-work necessary, and precisely what keeps it from ever being automatic: the made will not tend its own poison.
 The whole arc of the chapter can now be seen at once, and it bends back upon itself in a way that sets the agenda for everything that follows. The human began squeezed — an under-fitted body caught between its inherited equipment and a world that no longer accommodated it. Artificiality was its answer to that first squeeze, and a brilliantly successful one: the artificial food chain lifted it clear of the predation and scarcity that bear on everything still inside the web of life. But the answer did not abolish the squeezing; it relocated it. The runaway recursion that resolved the first mismatch generated a second — between the slow biological human and its own accelerating artifice — and this second squeeze, the chapters ahead will argue, is by far the graver of the two. The creature that built its way out of the first squeeze has been building its way into the last.
 Why the second is worse can be stated precisely, in the vocabulary Chapter I supplied and this chapter has extended. The first squeeze is a clash of configurations — one structure, the inherited body, against another, the structure of a changed world — and beneath both clashing configurations lies a shared order of principles: the same physics, the same logic of selection, within which the misfit can, in principle, be re-fitted by adapting, moving, or rebuilding. That shared substrate is what makes mismatches of the first kind resolvable in the end. The second squeeze is not like this. It is not a clash of configurations beneath a common order; it is a clash of the orders themselves — the enduring principles of the given world set against the enduring principle of a made one that has begun to set its own terms — and between two such orders there is no deeper court of appeal in which to re-fit them. A squeeze that has migrated from the layer of structures (the actual) to the layer of principles (the real) is a squeeze with nothing underneath it. This is the precise sense of the provocation the thesis will press in its later chapters: that the made has become a second nature — a technē that has begun to stand as a phusis, occupying the structural-virtual corner of the grid where it forecloses with the authority of the given while remaining wholly authored.
 And it is here that the double-edgedness turns from a danger into a trap. The structural register, recall, forecloses an action by removing it from the space of the possible; what it forecloses, then, includes the very counter-work — the regime of care — by which the pharmakon might be held to its remedy. The made now mutates at the speed of a platform update, while care still moves at the speed of biography and the slow handing-down of generations; and when the gap between those speeds is itself built into the structural register, the inversion Illich described stops being a contingent risk and becomes a structural certainty. The metaverse stage, on this reading, is not a break with the artificiality that came before but its limit case: the full realization of a tendency-toward-inversion that the pharmakon, the radical monopoly, the dialectic of enlightenment, Obara's self-petification, and the Confucian warning that 君子不器 — the gentleman is not a vessel, not to be reduced to a made instrument — each glimpsed in part and which the chapters ahead will read together. For now the chapter need establish only this much: that artificiality, in every register, cuts both ways; that remedy and poison are inseparable in it; and that which of them prevails is settled not by the made itself but by the counter-work we are able to bring to it. That is the threshold the metaverse will cross — and the threshold the rest of this thesis is written to meet.
 
# III. The Enlightenment's Disposition to Universalization
 Chapter II argued that artificiality is the human mode of survival, and that the world it builds is irreducible to the universe beneath it. That argument was made about artificiality as such — about what is common to every human world, from the Roman household to the Maya court to the digital platform. This chapter narrows the lens. It concerns one mode of artificiality among the many that human history has produced, and it advances a single thesis about that mode: that the form of world-making born of the European Enlightenment carries an intrinsic disposition to universalization — a tendency to overwrite the organizing conditions of every other world it meets, and to carry its own made world as though that world were the given universe itself. This disposition belongs to one mode; it is not a property of artificiality in general. Establishing that it belongs to one mode, and not to all, is the precondition for the squeeze hypothesis that the rest of the thesis develops.
 Two terms must be held apart from the outset, because the whole argument turns on the distinction. The universe, in the sense Chapter I fixed, is the given substrate — the Umgebung, the surrounding world that is there independently of any inhabitant, answering, in Rémi Brague's phrase, to neither a god nor a man. Universalization is something else entirely: it is a property not of the universe but of a world. It is what happens when one made world — one Umwelt, one inhabitant's worked-over surroundings — comes to treat itself as the Umgebung-of-all, the single given frame within which every other world must be measured and placed. The universe does not universalize; only a world can, and only by mistaking itself for the universe. When, in the chapters on the metaverse, I describe a made order that has begun to operate with the unanswerable authority of the given — a tekhnē that has come to stand as a phusis — it is the terminal case of exactly this movement that I will mean.
 A note on vocabulary follows from Chapter II and governs this chapter too. I do not use technology for the wide field of the made; that word has narrowed, in ordinary use, to a domain of devices, and the mode I am describing reaches into metaphysics, law, and the stance a civilization takes toward nature, none of which is a device. I use artificiality, in the broad sense already defended. And I retire the prefix neo-, which marks a thing only as coming after something else without saying what it is; where I need to name the most recent phase of the mode, I will call it metaverse-stage artificiality, naming the phase by its destination rather than by its mere succession.
 The argument proceeds in eight movements. The first establishes that there is a genuine plurality of human worlds, and recovers, beneath that plurality, the synchronic squeeze between a world and the universe that underlies every one of them. The second turns to collapse, reading it as that squeeze in its extreme form — a mismatch, not an unfitness — and locating in the survivors of collapse the regeneration base on which every recovery has drawn. The third earns the name: it argues why the mode in question is rightly called the Enlightenment mode, rather than Western or modern, and identifies the universalization of the sovereignty of the subject as its defining characteristic. The fourth denaturalizes that sovereignty by setting it beside ways of being a self — Buddhist and Daoist among them — that do not posit it, so that the sovereign subject is seen as one configuration among others rather than the human condition. The fifth and sixth give the mode its conditions of emergence — material in the fifth, nonmaterial in the sixth — without making either into a claim of superiority. The seventh meets the most natural objection head-on, that a mode which has universalized must thereby be the fittest, and shows why universalization is not fitness but its opposite. The eighth turns the mode's disposition inward, toward the pursuit of a life lived wholly as intended, and so hands the argument to Chapter IV.
## A. The plurality of worlds and the squeeze beneath them
 In the criteria of AOS in Chapter I, I already show the incommensurability of personhood across different human worlds as AOSs. Implied by such incommensurability is the distinct ways of organizing each AOS, including persons as its components. Historical accounts of cross-civilizational encounters help illustrate such plurality. The rejection of Britain's trade request by Emperor Qianlong of the Qing Empire to Macartney, while likely read as infamously arrogant and ignorant today (which is not necessarily wrong), some truth about my definition of plural worlds as AOSs is manifested in it: Not only the kowtow and tribute-vs-trade disagreement surfaces the difference in the operating logic from interpersonal to international relations between Qing and Britain, the claim that "Our Celestial Empire possesses all things in prolific abundance and lacks no product within its borders. There is therefore no need to import the manufactures of outside barbarians in exchange for our own produce" also shows Qing's AOS has achieved its own homeostasis (at least from the system operator's perspective) among the system, its members, and the local universe as its background environment, showing resistance against external interference on this homeostasis. This is not only seen in the international relations policy of the Qing Empire, but also not uncommon and other civilizations or even tribal societies (although this is not to make a normative judgment about it). 
 
 Philippe Descola's comparative anthropology provides one of the possible ways of comprehending the plurality of worlds with their underlying operating logic – the different ontologies on which the configuration and functioning of a world is founded. He identifies four schemas by which human collectivities distribute the two properties he takes to be basic — interiority (an inner life, intention, soul) and physicality (bodily form and material substance) — across the beings of the world (Descola, 2013). Animism grants a shared interiority but different physicalities: the Achuar and Makuna of the Upper Amazon treat animals and plants as persons with souls like their own, living in their own longhouse societies behind merely different bodies. Totemism shares both interiority and physicality within a class joined by common descent: in Aboriginal Australia a human group and its totem — the Kariera and their kangaroo — are alike the embodiment of one underlying essence. Analogism multiplies singular beings and reweaves them through correspondences, as in the Renaissance Great Chain of Being or the Chinese cosmos of resonances, where society, man, and world are known by analogy alone. Naturalism — the schema of the modern West, and, as Descola insists, the exact inversion of animism — does the reverse: it grants one shared physical nature, studied by the sciences, beneath a multiplicity of distinct human interiorities we call cultures. 
 These are not four cultural decorations of one underlying reality. They are four different ontologies — four different settlements of what kinds of being there are and how interiority and physicality are parcelled among them — and each furnishes a distinct foundation on which whole families of worlds, with their own persons, kinds, and modes of artificiality, can be built. The animist substrate grounds one range of possible worlds; the analogist substrate grounds another; the naturalist substrate grounds the worlds of the modern West.
 Naturalism, which feels to those inside it simply like seeing the world as it is — one real nature studied by the sciences, over which cultures lay their merely cultural interpretations — is itself only one ontological foundation among several, no more given than the others; and it is the particular foundation upon which the Enlightenment mode of artificiality, the subject of this chapter, raises its world. I take Descola's four not as a closed or exhaustive grid but as a demonstration that beneath the visible plurality of worlds there lies a plurality of ontologies, each capable of bearing many worlds, of which the naturalist is one.
 
 What this plurality of ontologies grounds is a plurality of actual worlds, not a plurality of possible worlds in the logician's sense, nor a plurality of cultural versions of one real world. The worlds are actual — really built, really inhabited, really structuring the lives lived within them — and they differ, as Chapter I put it, all the way down to their enduring organizing principles.
 
 Beneath this plurality lies a structure common to every world, and recovering it is the second task of this movement. Chapter I and Chapter II described a squeeze of one kind: the mismatch between an inhabitant and its environment that opens across time, as inherited structures come to lag a changed world. The Great Oxidation Event of Chapter I and the hominin mismatch of Chapter II were squeezes of this kind — dyadic, because they set one inhabitant against one environment; and diachronic, because the gap opens over time, the inherited equipment falling out of step with surroundings that have moved on. But there is a second squeeze, and it is the one this thesis is finally about. It is triadic and synchronic. An inhabitant of a world stands, at one and the same time, within two environments whose demands need not agree: the made world it actually lives in, and the given universe that world is built upon and never escapes. When the demands of the two diverge — when what the world requires of its inhabitant and what the universe permits begin to pull apart, down to their enduring organizing principles — the inhabitant is squeezed between them. This is the universe–world squeeze, and unlike the diachronic mismatch it is not a lag that time might close; it is a standing tension built into the very condition of living in a made world laid over a given one.
 Obara's ecological vocabulary lets us state the tension precisely. The human lives within an artificial ecosystem (人工生態系, jinkō seitaikei), a made web of provision selected for its function rather than ecologically given; and because the made web and the given one change at different rates and by different logics, a dis-synchrony (ズレ, zure) opens between them. Uegaki hardens this slippage into something sharper. Where Obara sees a zure, a gap that strains, Uegaki describes a fracture (亀裂, kiretsu) and even a severance (切断, setsudan) — a cutting-apart of what was once continuous (Uegaki, 2022a: 110). The made world can pull so far from the given universe that the connection between them, not merely strained, is cut. That cutting is the squeeze in its severe form, and it is the form toward which, this thesis will argue, the Enlightenment mode tends. [ADLER: I want you to revisit them both and engage them more deeply. Their description of what a dyssynchrony or fracture is more substantial than what is summarized here. in particular, uegakiJikoKachikuKa2014, "第二部 「人間的〈環境〉」の分析と人類史における連続性/非連続性" in uegakiJikoKanketsuShakai2022a]
 One qualification must be entered with care, because the whole moral of the chapter depends on it. To say that a world can be squeezed against the universe is not to say that it must be. The squeeze is a danger built into the condition of world-making, not a fate sealed by it; and human traditions have known the danger and built against it. The Confucian ideal of the unity of Heaven and humanity (天人合一, tiānrén héyī) names a world deliberately tuned to keep its made order answerable to the given one, rather than set over against it. The Daoist principle of wu wei (無為) — not inaction, but, as Joseph Needham renders it, refraining from activity contrary to Nature — describes a stance that, in his gloss on the classical sources — relaying Bertrand Russell's phrasing — produces without possessing, acts without self-assertion, and develops without dominating (Needham, 1956). Naming these ideals is not to claim that they were ever realized, or that the worlds professing them were thereby superior: the Chinese world collapsed many times — as the next section's Ming example shows — and its statecraft often honored 天人合一 and 無為 more in name than in practice. The point is only that traditions have articulated such restraint as an ideal; nor is the impulse uniquely Chinese, for many indigenous cosmologies enjoin a comparable binding of the made order to the given one. These are world-making traditions that hold restraint toward the universe as a constitutive value, and in doing so hold the squeeze at bay. The significance of this for the argument is exact: a mode of artificiality that abandons such restraint for mastery does not merely differ in style from these traditions; it courts the very squeeze they were built to prevent. The Enlightenment mode, I will argue, is such a mode. [ADLER: LOOKUP & GENERATIVE - integrate non-Sinic examples of attempts to harmonize human activity or society with nature in other cultural traditions, especially indigenous traditions.]
## B. Collapse and the regeneration base
 If the universe–world squeeze is real, then there should be a recognizable thing that happens when it grows severe — when the slippage between a made world and the given universe widens into a fracture. There is, and we call it collapse. I want to read collapse not as a verdict of unfitness on the world that suffers it, but as the universe–world squeeze in its extreme form: a mismatch between a world's organizing arrangements and the conditions the universe has come to impose, severe enough that the arrangements can no longer be sustained. The distinction between mismatch and unfitness is the whole of the matter, and the fall of the Ming dynasty, as reconstructed by the historian Timothy Brook, shows why.
 Brook's recent work assembles what he calls the longest series of prices in China before the eighteenth century, using, in his words, the price of rice to quantify the disaster and track its course through the dynasty's final decades (Brook, 2023: 106, 5). The series tracks the grain-price crisis of the 1630s and 1640s to its source, and Brook's verdict is unambiguous:
It was global climate, not global trade, that drove Ming grain prices to crippling levels. (Brook, 2023: 105)
 The Little Ice Age — a planetary cooling that shortened growing seasons across the northern hemisphere — pressed on a state whose granary system, the ever-normal granaries built to adjust the price of grain in times of dearth (Brook, 2023: 111), was never designed for a climatic shift of that depth or duration. The world's standing arrangements, adequate to the ordinary fluctuations of its given environment, could not absorb an extraordinary one. Brook is careful, and his care is instructive, to make the fall neither purely natural nor purely human:
The dynasty was not singlehandedly destroyed by climate, but its collapse cannot be explained in the absence of climate and human responses to climate. (Brook, 2023: x)
 This is the universe–world squeeze stated as historiography. The given universe shifted; the made world's arrangements lagged; the gap between them widened until, as the price regime broke, the political regime broke with it, and Beijing fell in April 1644 (Brook, 2023: 131). In his earlier survey Brook had already described the dynasty passing through a series of what he calls sloughs — troughs of climatic and epidemic stress — and concluded that their combination was what brought down the house of the Ming (Brook, 2010: 70–71, 255). The fall was a mismatch grown fatal, not a confession that the Ming world had been, all along, less fit than the world that followed it.
 Two consequences follow from reading the Ming fall this way, and both bear directly on the chapter's thesis. The first concerns what came after. The dynasty that replaced the Ming was not the Ming restored but a different world altogether. The Qing was a Manchu conquest régime that governed through what Pamela Crossley calls a simultaneous emperorship — a rulership addressed in Manchu, Chinese, Mongolian, Tibetan, and Arabic at once, each constituency met in its own idiom rather than absorbed into a single Chinese one (Crossley, 1999: 10–11). Mark Elliott describes the Qing order as one of ethnic sovereignty, securing a pax Manjurica rather than a pax Sinica, and enforcing its distinctness through the queue and the Eight Banners (Elliott, 2001: 2–6). The new order did not merely sit beside Han identity; it reshaped it by force. The queue edict compelled Han men, on pain of death, to shave the forehead and wear the Manchu braid, conscripting the very surface of the body into the conqueror's order; and the literary inquisitions (文字獄, wénzìyù) of the Qianlong reign policed the written word for disloyalty, destroying texts and authors alike in the course of the imperial book-collecting project. Identity was altered from the outside, by decree over hairstyle and costume, and from the inside, by control over what could be thought and written. This was a different adaptational operating system, in the sense Chapter I gave that term — a different settlement of persons, kinds, and rule, imposed in part by overwriting the markers of the world it replaced — not the previous one repaired. That collapse opens onto a genuinely different world, rather than restoring the old, is itself evidence for the plurality this chapter defends: if there were only one viable human world, collapse could only ever return us to it.
 The second consequence concerns what survives. Collapse, the archaeological record suggests, is almost never total. Joseph Tainter's analysis treats collapse as a rational economizing — a society facing negative marginal returns on its own complexity sheds that complexity and returns to a lower, more sustainable level of organization (Tainter, 1988). And what makes recovery possible afterward is that something is always carried across the discontinuity. Glenn Schwartz and John Nichols, surveying cases of regeneration after collapse, state the principle directly:
A crucial factor in regeneration is the survival of institutions or ideas from the era before collapse, supplying a base for the eventual re-creation of complex societies. (Schwartz and Nichols, 2006)
 Call this the regeneration base: the stock of surviving arrangements — institutions, techniques, memories, ways of organizing a world — from which the next world is rebuilt. Because collapse is almost never total (Schwartz and Nichols, 2006: 6), the base is almost always there. And here the argument reaches its hinge. The regeneration base on which the human species as a whole draws is not the residue of any single world but the plurality of worlds itself: the fact that, when one way of organizing existence fails, others persist from which recovery can be drawn. A mode of artificiality that universalizes — that overwrites the organizing conditions of every other world and presses toward a single planetary monoculture — does not merely dominate the others. It consumes the regeneration base. It narrows the species' stock of alternative ways of being to one, so that a failure of that one has nothing to recover from. Plurality, on this argument, is not a luxury of cultural richness; it is the species' regeneration base, and its erasure is the gravest cost of the Enlightenment mode's disposition to universalize.
## C. Why "the Enlightenment mode" [ADLER: Check this whole section and identify if it leans too much to critical theorist's dark diagnosis. Remember, the key core argument I try to make is one in alignment with Uegaki, which is about the elevation of the sovereign subject and reversing the direction of answerability -- from homo sapiens-humans embedded in their environmemts trying to attune themselves with their environmemt, to a hypostatized dis-embedded sovereign subject claiming/or not claiming what's owed upon them by the disenchanted world and universe, where artificiality is the means to answer to the subject (It is for the sovereign subject to choose whether it controls or perhaps does nothing, like nihilism or the latest "laying down" movement, tangping, in the modern world, the Sinosphere) -- perhaps this nihilist strand which is the other side of the same coin should also be mentioned, and this side also illustrates what the critical theorists have had a hard time explaining except appealing it to variations of "false consciousness (e.g., A&H's cultural industry as deception, Habermas' fragmented consciousness)." any gesture towards "domination" "control" etc -- the typical critical theoretical should be relativize as an endowment/entitlement that the sovereign subject can claim or not]
 I have been calling the mode whose disposition this chapter analyzes the Enlightenment mode of artificiality. The name needs to be earned rather than assumed, and earning it requires two steps: first to take, from authoritative accounts of the Enlightenment, the thread that this thesis means by the term; and then to show why that name is more precise than its two natural rivals, Western and modern. I take the first step here.
 A clarification must come first, because the word, left unglossed, will mislead. By the Enlightenment mode I do not mean the eighteenth century — not the Aufklärung as a period, not Kant's essay and the philosophes, not a chapter in the history of European ideas. I mean what Adorno and Horkheimer meant when they spoke of enlightenment in the widest sense as the advance of thought: a metaphysical disposition rather than a date, one that, in their words, has always aimed at liberating human beings from fear and installing them as masters of a disenchanted world (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 1). Enlightenment, in this sense, is not the property of a century but of a stance — the positing of the human subject as standing prior to, and over against, a given stripped of any meaning of its own, the world made into mere object, there to be known, calculated, and turned to use. The eighteenth century is where the stance reaches self-consciousness and gives itself its proudest name; it is the crystallization, not the origin, and not the whole.
 The historians and philosophers of the Enlightenment do not agree on everything, but a recognizable family of self-descriptions runs through their accounts, and three witnesses fix it. The Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy, summarizing the period's intellectual temper, characterizes the Enlightenment by its conception of nature as a very complicated machine to be understood and mastered, and by its suspicion or hostility toward received tradition, superstition, and prejudice, with its origin in the scientific revolution (Bristow, 2017). The second witness is Kant, whose short answer to the question what is enlightenment? has become the period's motto. Enlightenment, he writes, is:
man's emergence from his self-imposed immaturity… the inability to use one's own understanding without the guidance of another. (Kant, 1784: Ak. 8:35)
 The cure, in Kant's famous imperative, is sapere aude — have the courage to use your own understanding (Kant, 1784). The figure here is one of a universal human reason, the same in all rational beings, throwing off every external authority — tradition, church, custom — that had presumed to think on its behalf. The third witness is the most critical, and the most useful for my purpose. Theodor Adorno and Max Horkheimer, writing against the Enlightenment from within it, name the very thing this chapter is concerned with. The Enlightenment, they argue, has from the first sought:
the disenchantment of the world … to dispel myths, to overthrow fantasy with knowledge. (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 1)
 Its program, they continue, aimed at liberating human beings from fear and installing them as masters (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 1); and the form that mastery takes is total. What human beings seek to learn from nature, they write, is how to use it to dominate wholly both it and human beings (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 2). From this they draw the verdict that gives this chapter its central word: Enlightenment is totalitarian (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 4) — not in the narrow political sense, but in the sense that it permits no outside, no domain exempt from its single measure, until the control of internal and external nature has been made the absolute purpose of life (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 24).
 From these accounts I take one thread, and only one.
