The Enlightenment mode of artificiality, as I use the term, is characterized by its universalization of the sovereignty of the subject — the installation, as the measure of all worlds, of a single self-subsistent individual that stands prior to and independent of the given, owing obedience to no authority — tradition, nature, cosmos, or god — it has not itself endorsed. This imagined standing-free of the given — the subject's dis-embeddedness hardened from a useful abstraction into a supposed metaphysical fact — is what I will call its hypostatized dis-embeddedness, and trace through the chapters to come as the mode's hidden core. The nature-mastering, tradition-displacing universal reason the three witnesses describe is one way — the way the modern West most loudly championed — that this sovereignty operates: reason as the instrument of a self set prior to the given, mastery as one of its exercises. But it is not the only one. The same sovereignty turns, when mastery fails or wearies of itself, to its opposites — to the disengagement of nihilism, the disenchanted self facing the void it has cleared, and to the refusal of lying flat (躺平, tǎngpíng), the individual disposing of a life it takes to be answerable to no one by declining to strive at all13. These are no less the sovereign subject's than mastery is. Sovereignty of the subject is the root; controllability, which the following movements anatomize, is the operative means of its mastering face — one mode of the sovereignty, not its essence. One clarification guards the claim. Sovereignty of the subject is a practical-ontological posture, not a metaphysical theory of the self's substance: the Enlightenment thinkers disagreed fiercely about what the self is — Locke's memory-self, Kant's transcendental ego, Hume's self that is no abiding thing at all — yet agreed that the individual is the sovereign measure, owed its freedom. The posture is the shared ground beneath the metaphysical quarrels, and it is the posture, not any theory of the self's substance, that this thesis means. I do not take the whole of the Enlightenment — not its emancipatory politics, its science, or its critical self-awareness, each of which is real and some of which this thesis depends on. I take the specific structural disposition that the three witnesses, in their different registers, all describe: the self-subsistent subject set up as the universal measure, admitting no outside. That disposition is what I will trace, in the following movements, through its material conditions, its nonmaterial conditions, and its consequences. And it maps cleanly onto what has come before: the disenchanted nature of the Enlightenment is Descola's naturalism of the opening movement; its universal reason is the totalizing measure the movement on nonmaterial conditions will anatomize; its mechanistic mastery is the interventionist stance the movement on material conditions will trace; and the dialectic by which that mastery turns total is the universalization this whole chapter is named for.
 If the sovereignty of the subject names the stance, controllability names the operative principle of its mastering face — one of the directions in which the subject acts upon what it takes to be owed to it, not the essence of the stance — and here the term is borrowed with equal precision. Hartmut Rosa has argued that what distinguishes the modern relationship to the world is not a particular belief but a particular relationship: the drive to render the world verfügbar — a German word his translator, having weighed and rejected available, renders as controllable (Rosa, 2020: viii–ix). The world is to be made controllable along four dimensions: made visible, and so knowable; made reachable, and so accessible; made manageable; and made useful, pressed into service (Rosa, 2020: 14–17). This is not yet a reproach; it is the description of a comportment. To meet the given as that which is to be brought under control is one way of relating to it — and, as Rosa insists, not the only one, for there are relationships to the world built on attunement and responsiveness, on living within the given rather than mastering it. In its mastering face, the Enlightenment mode is the one for which controllability swells to fill the whole relation, so that the given appears as nothing but a field of what is, or is not yet, under control.
 Beneath the operative principle lies the root the whole chapter turns on, which the next chapter follows inward but which must be named here. That root is the sovereignty of the subject itself: the positing of the human as a self-subsistent individual, set apart from and above the given — what Uegaki calls the human's spatio-temporal self-subsistence (時空間的自立性, jikūkanteki jiritsusei), and what Charles Taylor,[ADLER: citation?] tracing its modern emergence, calls the buffered self, sealed against a world to which an earlier self had been porous. Controllability is how this sovereign subject reaches outward upon the given; the two drives that follow are how it presses. From that one root grow two drives, and the mode is their marriage. One is a drive of value: the conviction that the human bears within it a true nature, owed to it and not yet realized, to be brought about by remaking the given until it matches the ideal. The other is a drive of instrument: the reduction of the given to what can be measured, predicted, and worked. I develop these two engines, and the necessity of their convergence, in Chapter IV; here it is enough to mark that controllability is what they jointly yield — the value-drive supplying the ought, the instrument-drive the can, and the world remade between them.
 What makes universalization the present chapter's concern is that the controllability-relation does not stay within the world that bred it. A mode of artificiality is, in this thesis's terms, one actual world among many — one way of constituting a meaning-space within the given universe — and most such worlds have been content to be local, knowing themselves as one way among others of inhabiting the given. The Enlightenment mode is not. It carries its own made world as though that world were the given universe itself; and wherever it meets another world — another way of being a self, often a relational or world-embedded one — it does not encounter it as a peer but overwrites its organizing conditions, recoding them in the terms of the sovereign subject: the relational person remade as the self-subsistent individual, the world-embedded self as the buffered one. It does this by conquest where it must, but by seduction where it can — and the seduction is the more powerful, for the mode does not only impose the sovereign subject by force; it offers it. To a self bound in a web of mutual dependence it offers the sovereign individual's freedom, the relief of being answerable to no one — the very entrustment-felt-as-freedom that Chapter IV will anatomize. That a world can be overwritten by appeal as readily as by force is precisely why the mode is so hard to see and to resist: it arrives not only as a conqueror but as a liberator. That is the outward face of the mode, and it is what this chapter tracks: not the mode's internal dynamics, which wait for Chapter IV, but its comportment toward the worlds it displaces.
 Why, then, the Enlightenment mode rather than the more usual labels for this universalizing world? Consider first Western. The word names a region, and naming the mode by a region commits two errors. It essentializes: it implies that this disposition to mastery and universalization is somehow proper to the peoples or places of the geographical West, a feature of their character rather than of a particular historical settlement that arose among them and could have arisen, or be adopted, elsewhere. And it mislocates the mode in time and space, for the mode has already outlived Western political dominance. A future in which the leadership of this mode has passed to East Asia, or anywhere else, would not be the refutation of the mode but its fullest confirmation — the mode universalized so completely that it no longer needs a Western body to carry it. This is the precise point at which my question parts from a familiar one. Ian Morris and others ask which region leads — why the West rules, for now, and who may rule next (Morris, 2010). That is a real question, but it is orthogonal to mine. I am not asking which region runs the world; I am asking which mode of artificiality rules, whoever runs it. A change in the regional leadership of the mode leaves the mode, and its disposition, entirely intact.
 That this universalizing should have issued from the West, and not elsewhere, is the question the literature on the great divergence has long worried, and the thesis takes from it a deliberately modest lesson. Needham asked why the systematic, controlling science that remade the world arose in Europe and not in a China that had long been its technical equal; Pomeranz answered, for the economic divergence, that much was contingent — coal where it happened to be needed, and the windfall of New World land and labor — rather than written into any civilization's essence. To this material story the thesis adds a metaphysical layer: that what the West universalized was, before any particular technology, the controllability-relation itself; and that the universalizing was rarely gentle. Miura's reading of Locke shows the mechanism in miniature. The labor that mixes the self into the world converts the given into property; the earth is to be subdued and made over to the industrious and rational; and on that ground the lands of others — judged unimproved, merely natural — become appropriable without their consent, so that a theory of property does the work of a justification of conquest, the ownership of nature bound up with domination over alien peoples (Miura, 2006: 95–99). The overwriting of worlds is not a figure of speech; it has a documented history, and the controllability-relation is its principle.
 Yuk Hui gives this confirmation-by-adoption its sharpest form. What is spreading across the planet, he argues, is a monotechnology — a single technical-cosmological program presenting itself as the one rational way to inhabit the world — and its spread is achieved precisely under the banner of pluralism even as it dissolves the plurality it claims to honor. The slogan of pluralism, in his mordant phrase, has been accompanied by the disappearance of other cosmologies (Hui, 2016). Against this Hui sets the projects of cosmotechnics and technodiversity — the recovery of plural ways of relating the technical to the cosmic and moral order. For my argument, Hui's monotechnology is the terminus of the Enlightenment mode's disposition to universalize: the point at which the mode has so completely overwritten its rivals that only one way of world-making remains visible, and it no longer looks like a mode at all, but simply like the world.
 Consider, second, modern. This label fails in the opposite direction: where Western says too much, fixing the mode to a place, modern says too little, fixing it to nothing but a date. To be modern is only to be recent, to come after some earlier thing; the word is an empty temporal index that names no content. And because it names only recency, it generates an endless regress of qualifiers — modern, then late-modern, then post-modern, then post-post-modern — each marking a further interval without ever saying what the thing in question is. The mode I am describing has a content: a specific disposition, a specific stance toward nature and toward other worlds, traceable to a specific intellectual settlement. Enlightenment mode names that content; modern merely dates it, and dates it badly. For these reasons I keep the name. It is not a gesture of either praise or blame toward the historical Enlightenment, whose achievements and whose critics this thesis takes seriously in equal measure. It is a precise designation of one mode of artificiality, identified by its disposition, so that the disposition itself — not a region, not a period — can be the object of analysis.
 A discipline must travel with the name — the same one this thesis imposes wherever it speaks of the West. To call this the Enlightenment mode is not to say that the West is this mode, or that a civilizational essence drives some peoples toward control and spares others. The mode is a strand: one tendency, selected and radicalized out of a far more various inheritance. Its eponymous ancestors are conventionally named Platonic (the idea set over the given) and Aristotelian (the given known and ordered), and I will use those names — but as eponyms, not histories. Greek thought was never reducible to Plato and Aristotle, still less to idealism and empiricism; the sophists, the mystics, the atomists, the Stoics, and the Skeptics all stand off that axis, and neither Plato nor Aristotle was the modern idealist or empiricist the labels evoke. What the Enlightenment mode did was not to inherit the Greek mind but to select two strands from it, sharpen them, and universalize them, while the others — the traditions of withdrawal, of attunement, of dwelling within the given — were subordinated or forgotten. The mode is a distillation, then, and not a destiny; which is exactly why it might have been otherwise, and why to name it is to identify a tendency, not to convict a civilization.
 None of this is yet a verdict. To say that the Enlightenment mode meets the given as a thing to be controlled, and that it universalizes that relation over the worlds it meets, is to describe a comportment, not to indict it: the freedoms and the masteries this mode has won are real, as the chapters to come will insist as firmly as they insist on the cost. What the description fixes is only the shape of the thing whose expansion this chapter follows. Its eventual collision with the very given it declared itself independent of — the return, in Rosa's word, of an uncontrollability the mode can neither absorb nor abide — is what the later chapters will name the squeeze.
## D. The sovereign subject as one configuration among others
 To name the mode by the sovereignty of the subject is to make a claim that the mode itself cannot easily hear: that this sovereignty is a configuration, one settlement of what a self is, rather than the shape of the human as such. An assumption this deep becomes visible only by contrast — by setting it beside ways of being a self that do not posit it — and the contrast is worth drawing with care, because the careless version of it does real harm.
 Three configurations can be distinguished. [ADLER: VERIFY If typologizing these three configurations is necessary, I will. Otherwise, I want to avoid making empirical claims that are not necessary for establishing my argument. Or soften it so that these are examples that do not comply with a sovereign subject, instead of trying to typologize them exhaustively, because I have already typologized what is necessary and load-bearing to my argument. for every example given, LOOKUP with excerpt verbatim & CITE] There is the self-subsistent self that masters the given: the sovereign subject this chapter studies, which takes itself to stand over the world and works upon it. There is the self-subsistent self that withdraws from the given: the self of the renouncer traditions, which keeps its separateness from the world but turns from mastery to detachment, seeking release rather than control — the contemplative of the desert, the yogin of classical Sāṃkhya, the soul of Advaita seeking to disentangle itself from a world it deems illusion. And there is the self that is not separate from the given at all. [ADLER: This is an oversimplification. The point is not being unseparated, but being embedded. This implies a flat ontology. Embedded means there are still layers of irreducibility.] It is the third that does the decisive work, because it declines at the root the very separateness that the first two configurations share and the sovereign subject takes for granted. The Buddhist teaching of no-self (無我, anātman) holds that there is no abiding, self-subsistent self there to be sovereign in the first place — that what we call a self is a stream of conditioned arising with no owner behind it. The Daoist equalizing of things (齊物, in the Zhuangzi) levels the self with the myriad things, refusing it the privileged standpoint from which alone sovereignty could be claimed; the myriad things and I, in its formula, are one. Where the sovereign subject begins from a self set over a world, these begin from no such separation at all.
 [ADLER: same comment as the previous] The contrast must be drawn with one discipline above all, and the discipline is itself instructive: this is not a contrast between a sovereign West and a self-dissolving East, a romance this thesis refuses. The declension of the separate self has its own Western lineages, subordinated but real — the apophatic self-emptying of Meister Eckhart and the mystics, who sought the dissolution of the willing self in God; and Schopenhauer, who read the principle of individuation (the principium individuationis) as a veil over a deeper undivided reality, drawing the thought, by his own account, from Indian sources. One nuance must be kept in view, because it marks a genuine difference rather than a likeness to be smoothed over: the Western mystical self-emptying is characteristically an emptying into God, a transcendent ground that receives the dissolved self, whereas the Buddhist no-self is non-theistic, dissolving the self into no ground at all. Both decline the sovereign subject; they do not decline it identically. And conversely, the self-subsistent self has non-Western expressions of its own. What is at issue, then, is not a geography but three distinct settlements of what a self is — and the third shows that the separateness on which the sovereign subject is built is something some traditions decline outright. Set beside them, the sovereign subject ceases to look like the human condition and becomes visible as a selection: one world's way of being a self, not the world's.
 These traditions are invoked here for one purpose only, and it is important to be exact about what that purpose is not. They are not offered as a program to be adopted; this thesis counsels no one to become a Buddhist or a Daoist, and it does not imagine that the predicament it will diagnose is one an individual escapes by private enlightenment. The squeeze, as the later chapters will show, is a structural and collective condition, not a knot a single soul unties by adjusting its metaphysics of the self. What these configurations offer is not an exit but a vantage — the one vantage from which the sovereignty of the subject can be seen at all, because they are standing outside the water long enough to see that it is water. That is the whole of their work in this argument; the navigation they might suggest is reserved for the Conclusion, and held there under the same anti-prescriptive discipline.
## E. The material conditions of the mode
 Having earned the name and fixed the disposition, I turn to the conditions under which the Enlightenment mode emerged — first the material conditions, in this movement, then the nonmaterial conditions, in the next; the core of the comparative synthesis developed across both movements I first set out in Yang (2024). A caution governs both. To give a mode its conditions of emergence is not to call it superior. I am not explaining why the better mode won; I am explaining how a particular mode, with a particular disposition, arose where and when it did. The whole burden of the previous movement was that the mode's dominance is no proof of its fitness, and nothing in the account of its conditions is meant to take that back. What the material conditions explain is not a victory but a possibility — how a set of fortuitous circumstances allowed one interventionist world to break a constraint that had held its rivals in check.
 
 [ADLER: overall argument's simplified causality presented elsewhere, which I want you to VERIFY and if accurate, integrate: 貴族封建制⇒私的財産蓄積の条件 (Needham 2004)
重商主義⇒資本の迅速に蓄積するきっかけ (Needham 2004)
軍事競争⇒介入的科学技術の迅速開発に対するのニーズ (Needham 2004)
植⺠地支配と地下鉱物の開発 (Pomeranz)⇒成⻑負担の外部化⇒成⻑上限からの解放 (Miura)]
 
 Joseph Needham spent a scholarly lifetime on the question these conditions answer: why the systematic, experimental, controlling science that remade the world arose in early-modern Europe rather than in a China that, for most of recorded history, had been technologically ahead of it. His answer, across the volumes of Science and Civilisation in China, was never that Europeans were cleverer or their culture finer; it was that a chain of material conditions — geographical, hydrological, economic, and social — ran differently in the two civilizations, and produced, at its far end, two different relationships between knowledge and nature. Needham insisted, in the concluding synthesis of that work, that beneath the metaphysics and the science lie the material forces of climate, geography, and social integration (Needham, 2004). I take from him the chain itself — geo-ecology conditioning a mode of production, the mode of production conditioning institutions, institutions conditioning a metaphysics and a stance toward nature, and that stance conditioning a characteristic technoscience — while parting from him on what the chain is for, as I explain below. Table 3-1 sets the two modes side by side along this chain.
 
 [ADLER: I forgot where exactly it was, but I think it is important that Needham talked about the following -- VERIFY Needham + against third-party sources & CITE & integrate: 
 - the story of 揠苗助長 -- a farmer trying to pull up the staple to make it grow faster, which turns out to destroy it. This is his illustration of how the agricultural mode of production is related to the organicist ontology and interventionist axiology. 
 - On the other hand, he mentioned that the seafarers' and shepherds' mode of production requires high command and control and the object's obedience or subordination. This mode of production is the foundation of its mechanistic ontology and interventionist axiology. 
 - the west's inverventionist mode was initially realized through the command and control of chattel-slavery (not popular in China)
 - aristocracy and feudalism allows intergenerational wealth accumulation, later even strengthened by merchantilism (Chinese civil exam-bureaucracy + bureaucracy's 重農抑商 don't afford for such a strong intergenerational wealth accumulation -> weak culture of independent scientific studies outside of bureaucracy) + militaristic competition among aristocrat-feudal lords demands interventionist scientific progress (war conducted by the very few competing empires or with nomads, no prolonged aristocracy-based militaristic competition in China; science in China is state-governed program, mainly focuses on livelihood -> remain observational/non-interventionist) -> intergenerational wealth accumulation affords for scientific development for militaristic competition -> accelerating interventionist scientific progress]
 
Table 3-1. The two modes after Needham (2004): the chain from geo-ecology to technoscience. 

[ADLER: Here are two tables I made in other presentations, which I think are the most important; the other cells you created are not wrong but revise based on relevance:
