| 生産様式 | 自給自足の農業 | 畜産、人力航海 |
| 存在論| 有機論（陰陽五行など）| 機械論 | 
| 実践論 | 非介入（無為、調和など）| 介入（命令、服従など |
]

| Chain-link | China (organicist mode) | Europe / Mediterranean (mechanist mode) |
|---|---|---|
| Geo-ecological base | Continental, agrarian, dependent on large-scale irrigation. | Archipelagic and peninsular, maritime, oriented to trade. |
| Dominant mode of production | Hydraulic-agrarian, centrally coordinated; merchants subordinate.| Mercantile, merchant-adventurer, tending toward capitalism. | 
| Characteristic institutions | Centralized imperial bureaucracy that, in Needham's words, absolutely prevented the rise of the merchants and the coming into being of capitalism. | Autonomous city-states and an independent merchant class. |
| Metaphysics | Organicism and correlative thinking (理); a cosmos that is, in Needham's phrase, a mirror image of Chinese bureaucratic society. | Atomism tending to mechanism; a cosmos pictured as a machine of separable parts under externally legislated law. |
| Stance toward nature | Non-interventionist: wu wei, accord, harmony. | Interventionist: mastery and domination — so natural, Needham writes, to a people of shepherds and sea-farers. |
| Characteristic technoscience | Observational and correlative; propitious for the natural sciences, yet non-experimental. | Experimental and interventionist, pursuing the more complete the domination of nature, and with it the control of individual human behavior. |

 Two disciplines must be observed in reading this table, and they pull in the same direction. The first is Needham's own. For all that the right-hand column describes the mode this thesis is most worried about, Needham never reads the contrast as a verdict of East-good and West-bad. The left-hand column is a different configuration, not a moral victory; the organicist mode had its own limits, including the non-experimental character that, on Needham's account, kept its considerable natural science from becoming the experimental science of early-modern Europe. The second discipline is the one on which I must part from Needham. His grand narrative bends, in the end, toward teleology: the many ethnic cultural rivers of the world's sciences flowing at last into the one ocean of modern science, a single universal knowledge toward which all the partial knowledges were tending. That image is precisely what this thesis denies. There is no ocean toward which the rivers were flowing; there is one mode of artificiality that universalized, and presented its universalization as the ocean. I take from Needham the comparative chain — the material conditioning of a stance toward nature — and leave behind the teleology that would make the mechanist outcome the destination of all the others.
 That the European escape from the agrarian constraint was contingent, and not the unfolding of any civilizational superiority, is the burden of Kenneth Pomeranz's revisionist economic history, and it reinforces the modesty this movement requires. Western Europe around 1750, on Pomeranz's account, was a none-too-unusual economy; its most advanced region, England, was not obviously ahead of the most advanced regions of China. The Lower Yangzi delta, he shows, consumed about as much cloth per capita in 1750 as Britons did in 1800, which prompts his pointed reversal of the usual question — not why China failed to become England, but why didn't England become the Yangzi Delta?, locked like it into an advanced but involuting agrarian economy (Pomeranz, 2021: 23, 27). What broke England out, on his analysis, were two crucial discontinuities, one created by coal and one by colonies (Pomeranz, 2021: 7). Coal happened to lie in particularly fortunate places — near the surface, near water transport, near the manufacturing core — so that its exploitation owed as much to geographic accident as to ingenuity, an advantage Pomeranz calls fortuitous, and specifically English (Pomeranz, 2021: 66, 72). And the New World furnished a vast ghost acreage — land and resources, worked in great part by coerced labor, equivalent to tens of millions of acres England did not have to find at home — whose contribution Pomeranz reckons to have exceeded even that of coal (Pomeranz, 2021: 274–276). The escape, in short, was bought with a windfall of fossil energy and appropriated land, not earned by a superior way of being.
 If Pomeranz explains how the constraint was broken, the Japanese philosopher Miura Mitsuru explains why the breaking, once begun, entrenched itself — why a fortuitous escape hardened into a self-reinforcing mode. The mechanistic conception of nature, Miura argues, was not merely a theory but a convenience: by recasting nature as pure matter wholly devoid of anything mental, it freed the human, in his words, from the old religious view of nature, from taboos, reverence, and ethical restraint, and so answered precisely to the demand of capitalism and the modern state for domination over nature (Miura, 2006: 162). The metaphysics and the political economy locked together: a stance that licensed unlimited intervention served interests that rewarded it, and the reward entrenched the stance. The colonies completed the circuit, fixed as resource-supply zones within what Miura, following world-systems theory, calls a core-periphery structure that, he observes, still basically persists today (Miura, 2006: 233–234). And the whole arrangement carries, built into it, a vicious cycle (悪循環, akujunkan): a mode that, in Miura's phrase, at the very moment it achieves its specific purpose produces new evils requiring further intervention to manage (Miura, 2006: 156). Most telling for the argument of this thesis is the dependence engine Miura traces in a later essay (三浦, 2004: 15–16) [ADLER: VERIFY against the style guide to check whether name cited should be in original CJK language or romanized, then sweep-apply to all draft]: a region pinned as a supply-zone and market is driven to export great quantities of resources to obtain foreign currency, exhausting its own ecological base in the process — and the structure persists, as he puts it, basically intact, long after the formal empires that built it have gone. The material conditions of the Enlightenment mode, in sum, did not merely permit its rise; once in place, they wound it tighter, converting a contingent advantage into a self-perpetuating disposition to intervene. That self-reinforcement at the material level is the counterpart, in this movement, of the universalization the chapter is named for; the next movement turns to its nonmaterial engine.
## F. The nonmaterial conditions of the mode
 The material conditions of the previous movement explain how the mode could arise and entrench itself; they do not explain the feature that makes it dangerous. A mode of artificiality may be interventionist without being universalizing — may master its own corner of the world without claiming to be the measure of all worlds. What converts intervention into universalization is something at the level of how the mode knows: a specific structure in its way of relating thought to being. This movement traces that structure, and its spine is a single diagnosis, drawn from Roy Bhaskar's critical realism, to which I add one extension and around which I arrange a set of corroborating witnesses.
 Bhaskar locates the deepest fault in Western philosophy at what he calls the Platonic/Aristotelian fault-line — the inheritance, running from the two thinkers whose names this thesis uses as eponyms rather than histories, of a single recurring move — a diagnosis I have applied to modern knowledge in Yang (2024). He gives that move a name: the epistemic fallacy.[ADLER: revise the section by integrating the ontic fallacy. they are interdependent, dialectical pair, which is key to the hypostatized problem recurrent in this thesis. LOOKUP. here's a short explaination by NLM -- just FYI, DO NOT CITE directly -- citation must be based on verified source: In Roy Bhaskar’s critical realism, the **"epistemologization of being"** and the **"hypostatization of knowledge"** are two foundational, symbiotic errors that have paralyzed Western philosophy. They correspond directly to what Bhaskar calls the **Epistemic Fallacy** and the **Ontic Fallacy**, respectively. Here is how Bhaskar defines them and traces their destructive interaction through the Platonic-Aristotelian fault line, empiricism, and idealism. 1. The Core Definitions **The Epistemologization of Being (The Epistemic Fallacy)** This is the error of reducing ontology (what exists) to epistemology (what we can know). It is the assumption that statements about the real, independent world can always be translated into, or defined by, statements about human experience, perception, or conceptualization. It effectively denies that the universe has any structure or depth independent of human apprehension. **The Hypostatization of Knowledge (The Ontic Fallacy)** This is the converse error. "Hypostatization" means treating a concept or an idea as a concrete, objective reality. The ontic fallacy occurs when we take our historically transient, socially produced human knowledge (the *transitive* dimension) and dogmatically project it onto the universe as an eternal, natural, and unquestionable given (the *intransitive* dimension). It is the "esoteric naturalization of knowledge"—mistaking our current map of reality for the territory itself. 2. Connection to the Platonic-Aristotelian (PA) Fault Line The PA fault line represents the catastrophic failure of classical thought to bridge the gap between knowledge and being. Because Plato and Aristotle operated within "ontological monovalence" (a flattened view of reality that denied absence, underlying mechanisms, and emergent depth), they could not rationally connect universal, certain knowledge with the changing, empirical world. Because classical philosophy lacked the ontological depth required to ground science, subsequent thinkers were subjected to what Bhaskar calls the **"primal squeeze."** They were violently forced to close the gap between knowledge and reality using reductionism. Trapped on the PA fault line, philosophers oscillate endlessly between these two fallacies: 1. **Retreating to Epistemologization:** Unable to prove how our ideas map onto an independent reality, they simply deny that an independent reality exists outside our senses or concepts. 2. **Retreating to Hypostatization:** Reaching the dead-end of the epistemic fallacy, they swing to the opposite extreme, taking our humanly produced knowledge and declaring it to be the absolute, unquestionable structure of the cosmos. Bhaskar refers to these fallacies as a **"tacit complicity of opposites."** Together, they fuse the world and our knowledge of it into a single closed loop, producing "subject-object identity" and making it impossible to understand scientific change. 3. Connection to Empiricism and Idealism/Rationalism Both major wings of post-classical Western philosophy—Empiricism and Idealism/Rationalism—are trapped on the PA fault line, and both commit these twin fallacies in their own unique ways. How Empiricism Commits the Fallacies: **Epistemologization (The Epistemic Fallacy):** Empiricism reduces the world purely to what humans can observe. It claims that unobservable entities (like magnetic fields or social structures) are either fictions or just shorthand for human sense-data. It flattens the world into atomistic experiences. **Hypostatization (The Ontic Fallacy):** Having reduced reality to human experience, the empiricist must explain scientific laws. They do this by noticing "constant conjunctions" of events (e.g., whenever I drop an apple, it falls). They then take this purely human pattern of *experiencing* contiguous events and hypostatize it, projecting it onto the universe as a "law of nature." They mistake the human sequence of observation for the underlying generative mechanism of reality. How Idealism/Rationalism Commits the Fallacies: **Epistemologization (The Epistemic Fallacy):** Idealists and rationalists reduce reality to thought, reason, or concepts. They argue that the structure of the world is inherently logical or mental, effectively claiming that the universe is made of the same "stuff" as human cognition. **Hypostatization (The Ontic Fallacy):** Having reduced reality to ideas, rationalists take human mathematical models, logical categories, or conceptual frameworks and hypostatize them. They project these human constructs onto the cosmos as eternal truths. Plato’s transcendent "Forms," Hegel's "Absolute Spirit," and Descartes' "perfect deductive science" are all hypostatizations. They take the conceptual tools used by human beings to navigate the world and turn them into the literal, objective architecture of the universe. Summary To escape the PA fault line and these paired fallacies, Bhaskar argues we must adopt an **Ontological Turn**. We must firmly separate the transitive dimension (our fallible, changing, social process of producing knowledge) from the intransitive dimension (the deep, stratified, independent reality we are trying to know).]
 
The epistemic fallacy consists in the view that statements about being can be reduced to or analyzed in terms of statements about knowledge; i.e. that ontological questions can always be transposed into epistemological terms. (Bhaskar, 2008: 25) 
 The fallacy, in plainer terms, is the reduction of the question what is there? to the question what can we know?, so that being is silently rewritten as the knowable, and whatever does not fit our way of knowing is denied the status of the real. Bhaskar calls the result an ontification of knowledge — the dressing-up of our epistemic categories as the furniture of the world itself — and ties it to what he names identity-thinking, the refusal of any reality that does not answer to the knowing subject's own terms (Bhaskar, 2008). The consequence is structural, and it is the hinge of this whole chapter. A way of knowing that has installed itself as the measure of being:
inevitably splits reality into two: that which conforms to its criterion and that which does not. (Bhaskar, 2008)
 Here, at last, is the mechanism of universalization stated in its most general form. A mode founded on the epistemic fallacy cannot recognize another world as simply another way of being; it can recognize it only as a candidate for its own criterion of the knowable — and a failing one. What conforms to the mode's way of knowing is admitted as real; what does not is consigned to the unreal, the merely subjective, the superstitious, the not-yet-known. The mode does not, then, meet other worlds as peers and defeat them; it is constitutionally unable to see them as worlds at all. It sees only one reality — its own — and, scattered around the edges of it, error. This is why universalization is not an aggressive policy the mode might choose to drop, but a disposition built into its way of knowing: to know, for this mode, is already to totalize and to exclude.
 Bhaskar himself extends the diagnosis one step in the direction this thesis needs. Alongside the epistemic fallacy he names an anthropic fallacy — the analysis of being in terms of human being, the silent assumption that to be is to be as it is for us. The anthropic fallacy folds together an anthropomorphism (reading the world in the image of the human) and an anthropocentrism (placing the human at the center of what is real), and it admits of regional variants: any geo-historical variant of anthropocentrism — Eurocentrism among them — that takes one human community's way of being as the measure of being as such, and one that reaches an extreme form in modernity. I take up this opening and give the relevant variant its own name. The disposition this chapter is tracking is an enlightenment-morphism and an enlightenment-centrism: the reading of all reality in the image of the Enlightenment mode's way of knowing, and the placing of that mode at the center of the real. Universalization, named at the level of the mode's metaphysics, just is enlightenment-centrism — the anthropic fallacy run in the particular key of one mode of artificiality.
 A self-correction must be made at once, because the argument is poised over a trap. If the epistemic fallacy were simply a flaw in human reason as such, then every world would commit it, and the chapter's thesis — that this is the disposition of one mode among others — would collapse into a truism about the human mind. That is not the claim. The Platonic/Aristotelian fault-line is an inheritance of one specific intellectual lineage, the Western one; it is not constitutive of human reason in general. There is nothing in Māori or Amazonian or classical Chinese thought that runs the epistemic fallacy in the form Bhaskar diagnoses; those are different settlements of the relation between knowing and being, with their own structures and their own characteristic errors, but not this one. The anthropic in enlightenment-centrism is, therefore, not humanity's man but the Enlightenment's man — a particular, historically formed subject mistaking its particular way of knowing for the shape of being itself. The diagnosis is local even though its object is a mode that claims to be universal; indeed, that is exactly the pathology — a local way of knowing that has forgotten its own locality.
 Two guardrails keep this diagnosis from sliding into positions the thesis rejects. The first concerns the nature of the error. To say the Enlightenment mode commits the epistemic fallacy is to say it makes a projection — it mistakes its own made world for the given universe, taking the structure of its knowing for the structure of being. But a projection presupposes something projected upon; the criticism is therefore realist to its core. There is a universe, a given substrate with its own determinate character, about which the mode is mistaken — and the mode's error is precisely to have lost sight of it beneath the image of its own knowing. The charge is not that the mode believes in a real world; it is that it has confused which world is the real one. The second guardrail concerns the alternatives. When I speak of other ways of knowing surviving in other worlds, I do not mean a flat relativism in which every world's account is as good as every other's, and the very notion of a mind-independent universe dissolves. I mean other fallible ways of knowing the same one universe — partial, corrigible, sometimes wrong, but answerable, as the Enlightenment mode's way is also answerable, to a reality none of them invented. The plurality this chapter defends is a plurality of fallible perspectives on one real world, not a plurality of incommensurable realities; and it is just this realism that lets the chapter say that the mode is mistaken, rather than merely different.
 Why rest the diagnosis on Bhaskar rather than on the more famous critics of Enlightenment reason? Because three of those critics each name a real face of the same thing, yet each, for the thesis's purposes, stops short. Adorno and Horkheimer, whose verdict that Enlightenment is totalitarian opened this chapter's third movement, capture the pathos and the self-undoing of the mode — the way its drive to master nature recoils into the domination of human beings — better than anyone; but their account is ontology-shy, diagnosing a dialectic of domination without ever quite saying what, in the structure of reality, the mode gets wrong. Derrida names the formal shape of the splitting move — the logocentrism, the metaphysics of presence, the privileging of one term of every pair over its excluded other — with unmatched precision; but his analysis dissolves the referent, leaving no positively real excluded middle, nothing on the far side of the split but more text, where the thesis needs there to be a real universe wrongly excluded. Habermas names the social form of the encroachment — the colonization of the lifeworld by the imperatives of system, money, and administrative power; but his diagnosis is social rather than ontological, and the resistance he mounts to it stays, for the thesis's purposes, too local, a defense of one (modern, Western) lifeworld rather than of the plurality of worlds as such. Each of the three is right about something; none gives what Bhaskar gives — a name for the positively real excluded middle, a genealogy of the splitting move, a structural mechanism for it, and an account that integrates the epistemic, the ontological, and the stance toward nature in one diagnosis. That is why Bhaskar is the spine and the others are witnesses.
 And there are further witnesses, working far from critical realism, who describe the same projection from their own vantages and so corroborate it. Edmund Husserl, tracing the crisis of the European sciences to Galileo, charged modern science with a substitution — it substitutes the mathematically substructed world of idealities for the only real world, the actually experienced life-world — and named the result a garb of ideas (Ideenkleid) thrown over lived experience until the garb is mistaken for the world (Husserl, 1970: 48–51). That is the epistemic fallacy described phenomenologically. Heidegger named the same move twice over — as the mathematical projection of nature, the prior casting of the world as measurable that lets modern physics find only the measurable in it, and as the age of the world picture, in which the world is set up as a picture before a representing subject (Heidegger, 1977). And John Dewey, from American pragmatism, traced the whole disposition to a quest for certainty and the spectator theory of knowledge it underwrites — the picture of the knower as a detached eye before a finished, separate reality (Dewey, 1929). Phenomenology, fundamental ontology, and pragmatism converge, from three directions, on the structure Bhaskar names: a way of knowing that takes itself for the measure of being.
 It remains to say what the splitting does in the world, for the exclusion the epistemic fallacy performs in thought is enacted, historically, as a politics. Walter Mignolo has named the apparatus: a colonial matrix of power whose rhetoric of modernity — progress, development, science, salvation — is inseparable from a logic of coloniality that subordinates and erases the knowledges it meets (Mignolo, 2011). Boaventura de Sousa Santos has given the erasure its sharpest name, epistemicide: the killing-off of other knowledges across what he calls an abyssal line, the line modern reason draws between the knowledges that count as knowledge and those cast into the zone of the non-existent (de Sousa Santos, 2007). And Yuk Hui's [ADLER: cite] monotechnology, met in the third movement, is the same erasure seen from the side of the technical: the spread of one technical-cosmological program achieved through the disappearance of other cosmologies. The epistemic fallacy, the anthropic fallacy, enlightenment-centrism, the colonial matrix, epistemicide, monotechnology — these are not six different complaints but one structure described at six depths, from the most abstract diagnosis of how the mode knows to the most concrete record of what its knowing has destroyed. That structure is the nonmaterial condition of universalization; and with the material condition of the previous movement, it completes the account of how an interventionist world became a world that could not see any other world as real.
## G. Universalization is not fitness
 An objection now presses, and it is the most natural one the chapter faces. If the Enlightenment mode has spread across the whole planet, overwriting its rivals until little stands outside it, is that not the plainest possible proof that it is the fittest — that whatever its philosophical sins, it has simply outcompeted every other way of making a world, as the fitter organism outcompetes the less fit? The objection has the force of the obvious, and meeting it is the work of this movement. The reply turns on a distinction the objection runs together: between fitness to a world and fitness to the universe.
 Take first the easier reading, on which to be fittest is to prevail within the world the mode itself has made. On this reading the claim is empty — a circle wearing the costume of an argument. The Enlightenment mode sets the very standard of prevailing: it defines success as productivity, growth, measurable control, the conversion of the given into the manageable. To say that the mode which optimizes for those things is the one that best achieves them is to say nothing. Alfred Crosby,[ADLER: cite] the historian of ecological imperialism, supplies the principle that exposes the circle: biological and ecological superiority, he observes, is meaningless except with respect to a particular environment and a particular standard of fit. Change the environment, or the standard, and the ranking changes with it. The mode is fittest by its own lights; but its own lights are not the universe's, and the incommensurability of worlds established in the first movement means there is no neutral, world-independent scale on which the mode's kind of success counts as success as such. The circular version of the objection collapses into a tautology. [ADLER: Use a short illustrative case to make this more grounded rather than abstract. for example, flip the standard to biodiversity facilitation, the Amazonians may be the fittest compared to the rest of the world. But this must be verified against evidence, otherwise provide some other very empirically grounded evidence to illustrate, case to illustrate.]
 The harder reading is the one worth answering. Grant that the mode's internal standard proves nothing; is the mode not fitter to the universe — fitter, that is, by the one standard that is not of any world's making, the standard of endurance under real constraint? Here the question is asked in the universe's own coin, and the universe keeps only one ledger: what lasts. And answered in that coin, the verdict does not merely fail to support the objection; it reverses. For a mode that has universalized has, by that very fact, converted a plural biosphere of world-making strategies into a monoculture of a single mode — and a monoculture, by everything the science of resilience has learned, is not the fit form but the fragile one.
 The resilience literature draws the distinction the objection needs and lacks. C. S. Holling separated two properties long confused under the word stability: engineering stability, the speed with which a system returns to a single equilibrium after a small disturbance; and resilience proper, the capacity of a system to persist, and to reorganize, through disturbances large enough to change it (Holling, via Gunderson and Holling, 2002). A system optimized for the first is brittle to the second: tuned to one equilibrium, it has shed the redundancy and variety that would let it survive being knocked off that equilibrium altogether. The adaptive-cycle model that Holling and his colleagues developed makes the danger precise. A system that locks into its growth-and-accumulation phase — rigidly efficient, tightly connected, stripped of slack — enters what they call a conservation phase of brittle over-connection, and, in their stark formulation, the longer a system is locked in to its growth phase, the greater its vulnerability and the bigger and more dramatic its collapse will be (Gunderson and Holling, 2002). Brian Walker and David Salt draw the management lesson: a system cannot maximize persistence, adaptability, and transformability at once; optimizing hard for present performance trades away the adaptive capacity that future shocks will demand (Walker and Salt, 2006). Nassim Taleb gives the same structure its sharpest edge: suppressing volatility does not remove risk but stores it, converting many small, survivable shocks into one large, unsurvivable one, until a system of zero optionality stands maximally exposed to the disturbance it did not prepare for (Taleb, 2012). And Tainter's law of diminishing returns on complexity, met in the second movement, completes the picture: the universalized mode keeps buying control at a rising marginal cost, until the cost of holding the monoculture together exceeds what it can pay. [ADLER: Try to give a short example for each to make them not too abstract. For example, in the classical agent-based ecological model, if sheep somehow gained solidarity and drove away all the wolves, eventually the sheep would all die because the grass was being eaten too fast, beyond its rate of regeneration. Since the grass was all eaten, the sheep also began to die. So dominance over an environment is not fit but fragility.]
 Put together, these results invert the objection exactly. The plurality of worlds is not decorative variety that a fitter mode can dispense with; it is, at the scale of the species, precisely the redundancy and the reserve of alternative strategies on which resilience depends — the biosphere's stock of other ways to be, held against the day when the prevailing way fails. A mode that universalizes does not climb to the top of the universe's ledger; it dismantles the very thing the universe rewards. It mistakes its dominion over a world for fitness to the universe, when in fact, by liquidating the plurality that was the species' resilience, it has made the whole more fragile than any of the worlds it replaced. Universalization is not fitness. It is the manufacture of a single point of failure on a planetary scale.
 The fragility has a particular source, and naming it precisely matters, because it is easy to mistake the argument for a moral one it is not making. The mode appears to succeed only by continually externalizing its costs — discharging them onto a colony, a future, an atmosphere, a buffer somewhere outside the ledger it keeps for itself. I do not raise this as a moral indictment. Evolution is [ADLER: impartial? ~~built on cruelty~~], and the universe keeps no fairness-ledger; the charge against externalization is not that it is unjust but that it is structurally self-undermining, because the buffers it draws on are finite and it has no plan for their exhaustion. Clive Ponting, surveying the environmental history of civilizations, found the recurring pattern: a succession of ever more complex and environmentally damaging ways of meeting the same basic human needs, each escalation buying time by reaching for a larger buffer than the last (Ponting, 1991). But the buffers deplete. The ghost acreage of the New World was spent; the deep carbon that replaced it is being spent now; and the manufactured dependence by which the mode pins other regions as supply-zones — the engine Miura traced (三浦, 2004: 15–16) — is a relation, contingent and reversible, not an intrinsic superiority that could be relied on to last. A mode that endures only by finding an ever-larger outside to consume is fit only so long as the outside holds out, which, on a finite planet, is not a property the universe will reward indefinitely.
 The deepest form of the cost, and the one that ties this movement back to the chapter's beginning, is what universalization does at the level of a world's enduring organizing principles. To overwrite another world is not always equally costly, and the cost scales with the depth of the layer overwritten. At the surface — the layer of phenomena, of particular tools and recipes — transfer is nearly free: a technique, a crop, a device can pass from one world to another at little cost to either, and worlds have always borrowed at this layer. One layer down, at the level of structures and dynamics, transfer means a functional re-coding: an institution imported from one world must be re-fitted to the structures of the host, and the re-fitting exacts a real but bounded price. But at the deepest layer — the enduring organizing principles, the 道 of a world, its basic settlement of what there is and how a life is to be lived within it — there is no re-fitting, only replacement. To overwrite a world at this layer is not to adjust it but to abolish it, and to abolish with it an entire alternative way of organizing a world at all. This is what the Enlightenment mode's universalization does that mere borrowing or trade never did: it reaches past the phenomena and the structures to the principles, and there it does not exchange but erase. [ADLER: Perhaps engage the monoculture literature here. I also remember in our previous notes and discussion that we talked about recontextualizing colonization through stratified ontology. a replacement of ontology by a world onto another.]
 And this coheres exactly with the lesson of the second movement, closing the chapter's circle. There I argued that collapse is a mismatch, not a verdict of unfitness — that the fall of a world proves nothing about its worth, only that its arrangements met conditions they could not sustain. The fitness argument is the same coin's other face. Just as the collapse of the Ming proved it unfit only to those who mistake a mismatch for a judgment, so the reach of the Enlightenment mode proves it fit only to those who mistake dominion over a world for endurance in the universe. Dominance is not fitness, and collapse is not unfitness; both are phenomena of the squeeze between a world and the universe, and neither is the universe's verdict on the worth of a world. The mode's universalization, read in the universe's own coin of endurance, is not the sign of its fitness but the mechanism of a coming fragility — which is the threshold the chapter must now hand to the chapters ahead.
## H. From the mastery of worlds to the mastery of life [ADLER: same problem with the chapter opening it it currently means to over onto the critical theorists dark controlling view. Should be recalibrated to Uegaki's]
 The argument of this chapter can now be gathered. I began by establishing that human worlds are genuinely plural, plural all the way down to the ontologies on which they are founded, and that beneath every one of them runs a standing tension — the synchronic squeeze between a made world and the given universe it never escapes. I read collapse as that squeeze in its extreme, a mismatch and not a verdict of unfitness, and found in the survivors of collapse the regeneration base, the plurality of worlds itself, on which every recovery draws. I then named the mode whose disposition is the chapter's subject — the Enlightenment mode, a metaphysical stance of controllability rather than a date — and traced its conditions of emergence, material and nonmaterial: the fortuitous circumstances that let an interventionist world break the agrarian constraint, and the epistemic fallacy that turned its intervention into universalization, a way of knowing that cannot see another world as a world. And I showed why that universalization, far from proving the mode the fittest, is the manufacture of a planetary monoculture and a single point of failure — fitness to a world mistaken for fitness to the universe.
 One movement of the argument remains to be made, and it is the one that carries the chapter into the next. The Enlightenment mode's disposition is to master the given by rendering it controllable; and a mode with this disposition does not, in the end, confine its mastery to the world outside the human. The same drive that overwrites other worlds turns, eventually, inward — upon the one stretch of the given that is nearest and most recalcitrant of all, the human's own life. For the human life is not, in fact, lived as intended. The body sickens and ages against one's intention; others resist being made over to one's design; the given, in oneself as in the world, declines to be simply controllable. Uegaki gives this recalcitrance its name in the negative — the reality that no life is lived entirely as intended (意のままにならない生, i no mama ni naranai sei), the anti-intended in the self and in the world — and a mode for which controllability has become the whole of the relation cannot meet that recalcitrance as a condition to be lived with. It must meet it as a hindrance to be overcome. The pursuit of a life lived wholly as intended, of as-intended life (意のままになる生), is the form the mode's disposition takes when it turns upon the human itself.
 That turn is the subject of Chapter IV. There I take up what I will call the self-finishing tendency of human life: the drive, native to the Enlightenment mode but rooted in something older in the human, to complete and perfect the given self by remaking it — to close the gap between the life one has and the life one intends until nothing recalcitrant remains. The mastery of worlds and the mastery of life are, I will argue, one disposition working at two scales; and it is the inward turn, more than the outward, that drives toward the terminal form of the squeeze this thesis is finally about. For when the mode turns its demand for controllability upon a human life that the universe has built not to be wholly controllable, it sets the made and the given against each other at the very site of the self — and prepares the ground on which a self-completing, metaverse-stage artificiality will, in the chapters that follow, press the human into the final squeeze between the universe and the metaverse.
 
# IV. The Pursuit of Life-as-Intended and the Self-Finishing Tendency of Human Life
 Chapter III ended with the Enlightenment mode turning its demand for control inward, upon the one stretch of the given that is nearest and least tractable of all: the human's own life. This chapter follows that inward turn to its destination. Its subject is what I call the self-finishing tendency of human life — the drive to close the gap between the life one has and the life one intends, until nothing recalcitrant remains — and its thesis is that this tendency is not an aberration of the Enlightenment mode but its immanent completion, the form the mode necessarily takes when it is brought to bear on the self. To show this, I read the same phenomenon through three thinkers who saw it from three distances, and I arrange them not as rival theories to be adjudicated but as three faithful renderings of one thing, each true to the moment from which it was written. Theodor Adorno and Max Horkheimer saw the tendency from its darkness, as domination; Jürgen Habermas stood at its hinge, naming both the darkness and what the dark view could not see; and the contemporary Japanese philosopher Uegaki Takahide (上柿崇英) sees it from its brightness, as relief and liberation. The chapter's final movement shows that the darkness and the brightness are two sides of one coin — two appearances of a single real mechanism — and that the coin, seen whole, is the squeeze this thesis is finally about.
## A. A view from its darkness: Adorno and Horkheimer
 The darkest reading of the tendency, and the one against which the others must be measured, is the one Adorno and Horkheimer gave in the Dialectic of Enlightenment. Their thesis can be put as a single claim about reason itself: that what drives the Enlightenment is, at bottom, self-preservation (Selbsterhaltung), and that reason in its dominant modern form is nothing other than the organ of self-preservation refined into method. Self-preservation, they write, is the constitutive principle of science, the soul of the table of categories (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 68); reason has become the organ of calculation, of planning, whose principles are those of self-preservation (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 65). They find the formula already perfected in Spinoza, for whom, as they quote, the endeavour of preserving oneself is the first and only basis of virtue — a maxim they call, pointedly, the true maxim of all Western civilization (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 22).
 From this principle Adorno and Horkheimer trace three movements. The first is that the rupture between the human and a disenchanted nature makes reason instrumental. Once nature is set over against the human as mere object, reason's only office is mastery: what human beings seek to learn from nature, in the line Chapter III already cited, is how to use it to dominate wholly both it and human beings. Nothing else counts (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 2). The cost is borne first by the human's own inner nature, which must be regimented to serve the work of domination — industrialism makes souls into things (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 21) — so that the history of civilization becomes, in their phrase, the history of the introversion of sacrifice, a history of renunciation in which the self masters itself by mutilating itself, as Odysseus has himself bound to the mast to hear the Sirens and survive (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 43).
 The second movement is that instrumental reason, having no principle but self-preservation, cannot stop at any boundary; it totalizes, and in totalizing it hardens into a second nature (zweite Natur) — a made order that confronts its makers with all the opacity and inevitability of the given one. Enlightenment, on this account, is totalitarian as only a system can be (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 18), and its totality is driven by fear: nothing is allowed to remain outside, since the mere idea of the outside is the real source of fear (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 11). Adorno develops the point under the name of natural history (Naturgeschichte): the made world, grown total, presents itself as a nature from which there is no exit, until there is nothing outside any more, precisely because it is total, and nature turns into an irresistible parable of imprisonment (Adorno, Negative Dialectics: 354–361). Enlightenment, having set out to dispel myth, reverts to mythology (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: xviii): the made world becomes a fate.
 The third movement is that this second nature turns against the self that built it. The same instrumental rationality that mastered outer nature, having mastered inner nature too, becomes a force of self-destruction — for a reason that knows only domination must in the end dominate the very self it was meant to preserve. Enlightenment, they write, proved to be a destructive natural force no longer distinguishable from self-destruction; the two principles combined in a murky fusion (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 71), until the mode has made the control of internal and external nature the absolute purpose of life (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 24). The drive to preserve the self, carried to its limit, consumes the self. This is the darkness in its full depth: a reason that is nothing but self-preservation, a self-preservation that totalizes into a second nature, and a second nature that turns, at last, against the life it was built to secure.
 This reading has the force of its consistency, and that consistency is also its limitation. To make self-preservation the constitutive principle of reason as such, Adorno and Horkheimer must read the whole canon of Western thought as a single engine turning on that one axle: Hobbes's war of all against all, Locke's laboring proprietor, Bacon's knowledge is power, Spinoza's conatus, Descartes's nature reduced to extension — even, in the Excursus, Kant's categorical imperative — all become expressions of the one drive to secure the self by mastering what is not the self. The monism is the source of the book's tragic power; it is also its cost. For if reason is nothing but domination, then everything the Enlightenment also brought — the genuine extensions of freedom, the real abolitions of real subjections, the conviviality that some of its institutions made possible — must be either illusion or ruse, the cunning of domination wearing the mask of liberation. And that conclusion is too strong. The freedoms are not nothing; the person no longer owned by another, the body no longer broken by toil it cannot refuse, the mind no longer bound to inherited authority, are not merely dominated in a subtler key. A theory that cannot tell the difference between a real emancipation and a disguised domination has lost the resolution it needs to describe the present, in which both are unmistakably at work at once.
 The limitation is not a refutation. Adorno and Horkheimer saw something real — the dark face of the tendency is not invented, and the chapters ahead will insist on it as firmly as they qualify it. What is needed is not to discard the dark view but to find a vantage from which its truth can be kept while its monism is loosened: a vantage from which one can say that the tendency really does darken into domination, and also that it really does deliver liberations that are not illusions. That vantage is what the next two thinkers supply — Habermas by naming precisely the contradiction into which the dark view's totality drives it, and Uegaki by describing, from inside the present, the brightness the dark view was structurally unable to see.
## B. The hinge: Habermas[Adler: good. Just check for duplication with the newly updated previous chapters.]
 Jürgen Habermas stands at the hinge of this chapter's argument, because he names precisely the difficulty that the dark view runs into and supplies the thread that leads from the darkness toward the brightness. His diagnosis of Adorno and Horkheimer is exact. By making instrumental reason the whole of reason, he observes, they expand it into a category of the world-historical process — they trace it back behind the capitalist beginnings of the modern age, into the very beginnings of hominization as such (Habermas, 1984: 366). And a critique pitched at that altitude pays a price: the contours of the concept of reason are in danger of becoming blurred (Habermas, 1984: 366), until reason can no longer be distinguished from the domination it is said to be. The trouble is sharpest as a matter of logic. A totalized critique of reason — a critique that declares reason as such to be nothing but domination — must be made in the name of something, by some exercise of reason, and so saws off the branch it sits on. Habermas names this the performative contradiction inherent in an ideology critique that outstrips itself (Habermas, 1987: 127), and he is careful to credit Adorno with having seen it: Adorno was quite aware of this performative contradiction inherent in totalized critique (Habermas, 1987: 119). The dark view is not refuted by the contradiction; it is shown to be, by its own admission, unable to ground the standpoint from which it speaks.
 Habermas's way out is to deny the premise that reason is one thing. Instrumental reason — the reason of self-preservation and mastery that Adorno and Horkheimer described — is, he argues, only one complex of rationality among others; alongside it stands a communicative rationality, a reason oriented not to the control of objects but to reaching understanding among subjects, whose telos is agreement rather than mastery. The failure of the dark view, on this account, is that it does not direct our thought to the path that is nearest at hand — the differentiated analysis that would distinguish reason's several logics rather than collapsing them into one (Habermas, 1984: 382). Once communicative rationality is admitted, the freedoms the Enlightenment delivered need no longer be dismissed as domination in disguise: they can be the real achievements of a reason that was never only instrumental. This is the thread that leads toward the brightness, and it is the reason the dark view's monism could be loosened without being discarded.
 But Habermas does not only supply the bright thread; he also continues the dark one, and it is this continuation that the brightness chapter will inherit. For communicative rationality, on his account, is under siege. The systems that instrumental reason builds — the market steered by money, the state steered by administrative power — grow in complexity until they detach from the lifeworld of shared understanding that gave rise to them and begin to colonize it. Habermas names this the colonization of the lifeworld (Kolonialisierung der Lebenswelt). The rationalization of the lifeworld, he writes, makes possible a heightening of systemic complexity so hypertrophied that it unleashes system imperatives that burst the capacity of the lifeworld they instrumentalize — and he calls this, with deliberate echo of Adorno and Horkheimer, the irresistible irony of the world-historical process of enlightenment (Habermas, 1987: 155). The result, in the present, is not the false consciousness the older critique diagnosed but something subtler: in place of false consciousness we today have a fragmented consciousness that blocks enlightenment by the mechanism of reification; it is in this that the conditions for a colonization of the lifeworld are met (Habermas, 1987: 355).
 This dark thread is the one Uegaki picks up, and the connection is explicit rather than my imposition: Uegaki derives his own central concept — the autonomization (自立化) of the social apparatus — directly from Habermas's colonization of the lifeworld, naming the Theory of Communicative Action and the Kolonialisierung at the point where he does so (Uegaki, 2022a). The hinge, in other words, runs through Uegaki's own text. What Habermas describes as system colonizing lifeworld, Uegaki will take to its terminus — a society in which the apparatus has so fully absorbed the work of living that the lifeworld of mutual understanding has thinned almost to nothing. But — and this is what makes Uegaki the figure of the brightness rather than a second prophet of the dark — he re-reads the very uncoupling that Habermas diagnoses as pathology. The detachment of the apparatus from the lifeworld is, for the people who live by it, also and at the same time a liberation: it is what frees them from the burdens that the lifeworld of mutual dependence had always imposed. The same process is colonization seen from above and emancipation seen from within. Holding both of those readings together, without letting either cancel the other, is the work of the next movement.
 Before turning to Uegaki, I should say plainly why the thesis leads with him and not with a more familiar tradition that runs alongside his. That tradition has a clear lineage — Max Weber, then Adorno and Horkheimer, then, in our own moment, Hartmut Rosa — and what unites the three across a century is that each diagnoses the modern predicament as a matter of the subject's relation to a disenchanted world of objects. Weber gave the lineage its founding term in the disenchantment of the world (die Entzauberung der Welt), the draining of intrinsic meaning from things until what remains is a field of the calculable14. Adorno and Horkheimer radicalized the thought, making the disenchantment of nature the engine of an instrumental reason whose whole content is domination — what human beings seek from nature, in the line already cited, is how to use it to dominate wholly both it and human beings; nothing else counts (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 2). And Rosa gives the lineage its late-modern form: the world rendered controllable (verfügbar) in all its aspects (Rosa, 2020: viii), a controllability whose recoil he names the monstrous return of the uncontrollable15. Across all three the diagnosis is a stance toward the object: disenchant it, master it, render it available — and forfeit something in the relation. This lineage is not a foil to be refuted; the thesis draws on it, and most directly on Rosa, whose vocabulary of controllability named the principle of the mode in Chapter III and whose returning uncontrollable will supply the universe-side image of the squeeze. But it works one pole, and the thesis's argument turns on the other. The question Uegaki asks is not what the disenchanting subject does to the world, but what the liberated subject takes itself to be, and therefore to demand: it is an anthropology of freedom (自由の人間学), a diagnosis of the subject's self-constitution. Where Weber, Adorno, and Rosa describe a relation to objects, Uegaki describes an elevation of the subject; and it is the elevated subject, not the disenchanted object, whose collision with the given this thesis will call the squeeze.
 The relation between the two, once the axes are laid across each other, is one of genus and species. Rosa's axis runs from the controllable to the uncontrollable; Uegaki's from the as-intended to the anti-intended; and the as-intended is the broader category, because it annexes much of what Rosa files under the uncontrollable16. Rosa's controllability is, in the vocabulary this chapter is building, one engine of the as-intended life — the instrumental, appropriative engine that renders the object available, the Aristotelian pole whose practical signature is Bhaskar's epistemic fallacy. Uegaki's as-intended life is the whole of which that engine is a part: it carries both the instrumental engine and the idealist, soteriological engine of the elevated subject — the Platonic pole, the endowed nature that the object-pole tradition does not theorize because its gaze is fixed on the object. To lead with Rosa would be to mistake a part for the whole, and to leave the thesis sounding like one more critique of controllability. To lead with Uegaki is to keep the subject in view, and so to be able to say what neither the disenchantment lineage nor the controllability lineage can: that the squeeze is not first a fact about a world made too available, but about a subject that has taken its as-intended life to be its birthright, and so reads the given — whatever in existence will not answer to its 意 [ADLER: CJK problem. use "intention"] — as an oppression to be overcome. Rosa names the symptom; Uegaki names the cause; and the thesis leads with the cause.
 There is a deeper reason still, and I offer it as a hypothesis rather than a demonstration. It is no accident, I suspect, that the elevation of the subject is the one thing this Western lineage does not theorize. Weber, Adorno, and Rosa are themselves heirs of the Enlightenment world, and the self-subsistent subject is the very air they breathe; it is the hardest thing of all to see, because it is the standpoint from which they see everything else. They can describe with great precision what the subject does to the world — disenchant it, master it, render it available — because that is an object held before the gaze. But the subject-pole itself, the prior elevation that makes the whole stance possible, lies behind the gaze rather than before it, and a tradition formed inside that elevation has no easy vantage from which to make it visible as one settlement among others. The vantage that does make it visible tends to come from those who are heirs, however partially, to another world — for whom the Enlightenment subject is not simply the shape of the human but a particular shape, recognizable as particular because it can be set beside another. This is not a claim of pristine exteriority; no such standpoint exists. The everyday locality in which a contemporary Japanese or Taiwanese thinker lives is already, in Chapter III's sense, heavily overwritten by the Enlightenment world — universalization has seen to that. The vantage is rather that of one who stands at the seam: inside the mode enough to know it from within, and heir to another world enough to feel its edge. It is from that seam, I think, and not from the comfortable interior, that the air becomes visible as air. That Uegaki writes from Japan, and that this thesis is written from Taiwan, is in this respect not incidental to the argument but a condition of its being seen at all.
## C. A view toward its brightness: Uegaki [ADLER: cross chapter check to see if there is redundancy with the newly updated chapter 3]
 The third thinker writes from inside the present that Adorno and Horkheimer could only foresee and Habermas could only begin to diagnose. Uegaki Takahide, in his study The Formation of the Self-Finishing Society (〈自己完結社会〉の成立, 2022), describes the same tendency the dark view described, but from its bright face — not as a cage closing but as a burden lifting. The difference is not that Uegaki is naïve about the cost; it is that he writes from a vantage from which the brightness is undeniable, and insists that a theory which cannot see it has missed something real. Before the analysis, the plain version of his claim should be stated, because it is simpler than its apparatus makes it sound: modern people increasingly live lives in which the things that used to be hard — securing a livelihood, being recognized, getting through the day's needs — are done for them by a vast apparatus, so that they experience as freedom what an earlier generation experienced as dependence; and this experience, Uegaki insists, is not an illusion. The freedoms are real. They are simply purchased at a price the bright face cannot see, and which it is the work of this chapter to name.
### (A) Three terms
 Three of Uegaki's terms carry the argument, and each must be set down with care, because each has been mistranslated in ways that distort the claim. [ADLER: introduce the concept directly. do not lead with "mistranslation". nuances of translation should be kept in footnotes. as we already discussed, Uegaki is referring to the West as its referrent, so the subject of concern should be the referrent.] The first is as-intended life (意のままになる生). The temptation is to render 意 as will and the phrase as life-as-willed, but this narrows it fatally. 意 is the whole mind-heart — what Kant [ADLER: cite] gathered under Gemüt, the seat at once of cognition, of feeling, and of striving, and what Brentano [ADLER: cite] marked as the intentional character of the mental, its directedness upon objects. As-intended life is therefore not merely life as one wills it in the narrow voluntarist sense, but life as it answers to the whole intending mind — to what one knows, feels, and wants, taken together [ADLER: cognition, affection, conation]. Its opposite is the anti-intended (意のままにならない): whatever does not answer to the intending mind, whatever resists. And the sharpest name Uegaki gives the ideal is the human-worldview (世界観＝人間観) [ADLER: This is one of Uegaki's brilliance or wisdom lies. He coined this term because when we talk about world view in English, we are talking about "different ways of viewing the world". but what is entailed in that but always hidden, hardly ever surfaced is "what kind of human is making such worldview"-- therefore there is always this "human view entailed world view" in which is about how is a human viewed or how do we view human just like how do we view the world] of [ADLER: dis-embedded life ~~limitless life~~] (〈無限の生〉)  — where limitless means not immortality but the limitless actualization of an imagined ideal upon reality, the project of bending the given without remainder to the shape the mind intends. Its true opposite is [ADLER: embedded life ~~finite life~~] (有限の生): a life that affirms its [adler: embeddedness ~~limits — the body, the other, mortality — ~~] rather than treating them as hindrances to be overcome.
 The second term is the social apparatus (社会的装置). By this Uegaki means the whole artificial system to which one entrusts (委ねる) the work of living — the markets, bureaucracies, infrastructures, and now digital platforms that take over the tasks a person once had to perform within a web of mutual dependence. The decisive point, and the one that separates Uegaki's analysis from Adorno and Horkheimer's, is the phenomenology of the apparatus: it is felt not as domination but as relief. To entrust one's livelihood to an economy, one's safety to a state, one's needs to a service, is experienced as a liberation from the burdens that the old face-to-face dependencies imposed. Whatever the apparatus is doing structurally, what it feels like from inside is the lifting of a weight. Any reading that imports the vocabulary of domination at this point — that insists the relief is really subjection — has already refused the datum Uegaki is asking us to hold.
 The third term is self-finishing (自己完結化), and it names the destination. A life becomes self-finishing when it is closed into itself, made complete-in-itself, able to run its loop — to meet its needs, secure its recognition, fill its days — without essential reference to other people. The apparatus is what makes this closure possible: by absorbing the functions that once required others, it lets a life be lived, in principle, alone. The term carries a deliberate double meaning that the English self-finishing preserves: to finish is both to complete and to bring to an end, and the self that finishes itself in the first sense risks finishing itself off in the second. The completion is also a kind of self-undoing. But — and this is Uegaki's brightness, held to the last — the people living the self-finishing life are not dupes. Through the apparatus's absorption of the anti-intended, they really do hold the modern values: freedom, equality, autonomy, conviviality, held as users (〈ユーザー〉) of the apparatus that delivers them. Against Adorno and Horkheimer, these are not illusions. The life of a person today is, by these measures, really freer than the life of a person a century ago. That is the datum. The chapter's task is to understand what it costs.
### (B) The soteriological structure
 The modern values, Uegaki argues, are not held flatly, as bare political facts; they are grounded ontologically, given a metaphysical weight that turns them from arrangements into destinies. And the structure of that grounding, on his reading, is a secularized salvation-history — the Christian drama of fall and redemption transposed into a worldly key. Three terms carry the structure. There is ontological freedom (存在論的自由): the conviction that the human has a true nature owed to it, a way it is meant to be — what Uegaki calls the human as it ought to be (本来の人間) or one's true endowed nature (約束された本来性), and what the German tradition named the vocation of the human (Bestimmung des Menschen). This, Uegaki observes, derives from Renaissance Christianity — it is the imago Dei and the covenant, the promise of a redeemed humanity, secularized into a this-worldly entitlement. There is ontological oppression (存在論的抑圧): the condition of being held back from that true nature — the secular form of the Fall, appearing as Rousseau's chains, as Marx's estrangement, as Kant's self-incurred immaturity. And there is liberation (解放): the movement from oppression to freedom, the secular form of redemption. Adorno and Horkheimer themselves belong to this lineage; their critique is itself a redemption-story, which is part of why it could not see its own ground.
### (C) The slippage from a political into an ontological program
 The danger lies in a slippage from the political register to the ontological one, and the case of human rights shows it most clearly. At the political stage, ontological freedom overlaps with something this thesis wholly affirms: the right not to be subjected to unjust power, the claim of the enslaved, the colonized, the disenfranchised against those who hold them down. So far the ideal does real and legitimate work. But when the same ideal is read ontologically — as the promise of a true nature owed to the self — the right against unjust subjection slides into a right against the given as such: a claim not merely against the tyrant or the master but against any limit whatever, including the limits the universe imposes on every life. Uegaki marks the fork with two phrases: the reality-accompanying ideal (現実に寄り添う理想), which works within the given to better it, and the reality-negating ideal (現実を否定する理想), which sets itself against the given as such. The slide from the first to the second is, in the critical-realist vocabulary this thesis has used, an instance of what Bhaskar called the ontic fallacy — the compulsive determination of knowledge by being, the taking of a hypostatized ideal for a fact of the world (Bhaskar, 1993: 4). Here the hypostatized idea is the human as it ought to be (本来の人間), the anthropic ideal, taken as though it were a reality the given is failing to deliver — so that the given itself stands convicted of withholding what was owed. [ADLER: Add a footnote of the detailed argumentation Uegaki made and also how I read it from a critical realist's perspective, which I think should be in the previous drafts. `無限の生 being unique to the enlightenment tradition, whereas 有限の生 being the default of most civilizations; 無限の生 vs 有限の生 views influence how one perceives the anti-intended (my take is that while Uegaki tries to distinguish the two, a Westerner probably would say he doesn't fully justify it, since he's a japanese living with 有限の生 as default):

### (D) The problem of others
 This soteriological structure runs into two limits that the rest of the analysis turns on, and they concern the two things most stubbornly anti-intended: other people, and one's own body. The first limit is the problem of others. Other people are, finally, the anti-intended as such — they do not exist, become, or act as one intends, and no project of self-realization can simply will them into compliance. The soteriologist's solution is an axiological universalization: the freedom claimed for oneself is extended, in principle, to all, so that each is to be emancipated into their own true nature. But this is, Uegaki argues, naïve, because it leaves the anti-intended unaddressed rather than resolved. [Adler: we have to acknowledge that these thinkers have done their best to try to resolve this problem beyond just claiming it. For example, their political theories, the division of power, and the concept of human rights are all real fruits of their hardships. We need to integrate this in a way that acknowledges their work without rejecting our argument. In fact, the artificiality of these political theories and institutions is what we argue recoils in later arguments. Maybe in the next section, confluence of two engines?] If everyone is to be freed from the burden of the anti-intended, who carries the burden? The right not to bear the weight of others becomes, in the limit, part of what one is owed as an irreplaceable self (かけがえのないこの私), [ADLER: These are equally important. Let's try to find a way to integrate them: the me just-as-I-am (ありのままの私) and (こうでなければならない私 -- translation absent), cf. https://schs.kyojinnokata.net/keyword/16-12.html] — and the other, precisely as anti-intended, comes to figure as a high-risk, high-burden being that oppresses the irreplaceable self (意のままにならない他者). Emancipation, pressed to the ontological register, turns the other from a fellow into an encumbrance.
### (E) The problem of the body
 The second limit is the problem of the body. Western thought has, since the Greeks, ranked soul above body, and the body is, after the other, the most intimate site of the anti-intended (意のままにならない身体) — it sickens, ages, tires, desires against one's intention, and finally dies. The soteriological logic, pressed to its limit, arrives at a figure Uegaki names the mind-as-being (思念体)[^iv-tulpa]: a self that has shed even the body, a pure intending mind freed at last from the recalcitrance of flesh. This is, in effect, the res cogitans of Descartes returned as an aspiration — the thinking thing without extension — and it wears, in its modern form, the look of a secular Gnosticism, salvation as escape from the body rather than redemption of it. (The identification of Uegaki's mind-as-being with the Cartesian res cogitans is mine, not his; and the rendering of 思念体 is provisional — it is the limit-form toward which the disembodiment of the as-intended life tends.) These two limits — the other reduced to an encumbrance, the body to a hindrance to be escaped — are not incidental difficulties. They are where the soteriological engine, left to itself, drives; and they are why, as the next section shows, it cannot run without the second engine, the instrumental one, to carry the burden it cannot otherwise discharge.
### (F) The confluence of the two engines
 Here is the spine of the chapter, the claim that everything before it has been preparing and everything after it will draw on. There are two engines in the Enlightenment mode's drive toward the as-intended life, and they are not rivals but partners — two expressions of a single root, which converge not by historical accident but by internal logic. The first is the engine just described: the soteriological value-engine, the drive that supplies the ought. It says that the human has a true nature owed to it, that the given withholds it, and that the given must therefore be remade until the promise is kept. Read in the critical-realist vocabulary, this is the Platonic pole, and it runs on the ontic fallacy — the hypostatized ideal taken for a reality the world is failing to deliver. The second is the engine Chapter III anatomized: the instrumental engine, the drive that supplies the can. It reduces the given to what can be measured, predicted, and worked, so that it may be brought under control. This is the Aristotelian pole, and it runs on the epistemic fallacy — being reduced to the knowable, the knowable to the calculable, the calculable to the controllable.
 That these two are one at the root can be shown directly. Both grow from the single posture Chapter III named as the root of the whole mode — the sovereignty of the subject: the human posited as standing prior to, and over against, a disenchanted given, what Uegaki called the spatio-temporal self-subsistence (時空間的自立性) of the subject. From that one posture, two demands follow. Because the self stands over the given, the given owes the self its true nature (the value-engine); and because the given is mere object before the self, it lies open to calculation and control (the instrumental engine). The ought and the can spring from the same separation. And this is why they must converge rather than merely coexist: the value-engine's promise cannot be kept without the instrumental engine's apparatus. To deliver the true nature owed to every self — to free each person from the anti-intended other and the anti-intended body — requires that the burden of the anti-intended be borne by something. The soteriological engine, by itself, can only universalize the demand; it cannot discharge it. Only the instrumental engine, by building an apparatus that absorbs the anti-intended, can make the promise good. The value-engine writes the cheque; the instrumental engine is the only account that can cover it. Their convergence is not a contingent alliance but a structural necessity: the one cannot complete itself without the other.
 [Adler: Check for duplication with the newly updated previous chapters.] Bhaskar's own formulations anchor the two fallacies and their kinship. The epistemic fallacy, he writes, results in the systematic dissolution of the idea of a world — an intransitive, mind-independent reality — independent of but investigated by science (Bhaskar, 2008: 26). The ontic fallacy is its mirror: an ontologization of knowledge, in which our categories are projected onto being as hypostatized ideas, so that what we take to be there is governed by what we think rather than the reverse (Bhaskar, 1993: 4). And the two, far from being independent errors, characteristically presuppose each other (Bhaskar, 2009: 16): the reduction of being to the knowable and the projection of the known onto being are two moments of a single failure to keep being and knowledge distinct. Both, on Bhaskar's genealogy, route back through the same lineage — to Parmenides and Plato, the same fault-line Chapter III located at the root of the mode. And both, in the same way, foreclose the intransitive real: the value-engine by convicting the given of failing to deliver an ideal, the instrumental engine by admitting of the given only what its method can grasp. Between them they leave no standing-room for a universe that is simply there, neither owing the self its true nature nor reducible to the self's calculations. That foreclosure — the squeezing-out of the intransitive real by both engines at once — is what the chapter's final movement will show to be the squeeze itself.
### (G) The migration from the conventional to the structural
 How the apparatus discharges the burden of the anti-intended can be described precisely in the vocabulary Chapter II established. The apparatus does not dominate; it receives what one entrusts (委ねる) to it. And what is entrusted is offloaded along two axes. Horizontally, the apparatus takes over the functions once carried by the people around one — the roles, the trust, the forgiveness that a web of mutual dependence supplied; one no longer needs a neighbor's goodwill where a service will do. Vertically, it takes over the deeper supports of a life — the realization of survival (one's livelihood), one's existence-in-society (one's standing as a recognized person), and succession (the handing-on of life across generations). As these are offloaded, the values once held in a fabric of mutual dependence come to be held instead, in Uegaki's vivid image, as users hanging from (ぶら下がっている) the apparatus — freedom, equality, autonomy, and conviviality enjoyed as [adler: features or services of the system] rendered rather than as relations sustained. And the directionality of this offloading is exactly the migration Chapter II's grid charts: from the conventional register, where the made holds because people keep enacting it, to the structural register, where it holds because it is built into the operating environment and runs whether or not anyone attends to it. The life of mutual dependence was conventional; the life hung from the apparatus is structural.
 This migration is not a thought-experiment; it has a legible history, and Japan furnishes an unusually clear case of it — not because Japan is the cause of the tendency, nor because the case is parochial, but because there the compression of the change into roughly four generations, from the Meiji era to the present, makes visible what elsewhere is spread too thin to see. (The plurality of worlds established in Chapter III is untouched by this: Japan is one legible instance of a migration the mode drives wherever it reaches, not the template of a single world.) Uegaki narrates the change as a sequence of five periods, and as a shift in the figure of the self — from the Traveller (旅人), who leaves a home port for a destination, to the Drifter (漂流人), who has lost port, destination, and compass alike. Table 4-1 sets out the arc.
Table 4-1. The migration from mutual dependence to the self-finishing society, after Uegaki (2022): five periods in the Japanese case.

| ------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |

| 1945–1970    | Traveller (旅人)                                                                   | Conventional → market/bureaucratic [Adler: a continuation of the previous cell. This is on the move to the structural: the automatic, escalating financial activities and bureaucratic processes as coded by its protocols. | 1995–2010    | Drifter (漂流人)                                                                    | [Adler: Still, in the gradation, the continuum of becoming structural.] (informatization)                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             | The infosphere (情報世界) as a structural environment; port, destination, and compass all lost. The employment ice age; recognition-anxiety; the mobile phone.                                                                                     |
| 2010–present | (self-finishing)                                                                 | [Adler: Still, in the gradation, the continuum of becoming structural.]                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               | Deep dependence on the social apparatus — IT, robotics and AI, life-manipulation; the necessity of relating to flesh-and-blood others lost. Single-person households the most common type; solitary death; an ethic of non-intervention (不介入). |

 Read off the table, the arc is unmistakable: a life once held together by the enacted bonds of others is, period by period, rebuilt as a life hung from a structural apparatus that asks no enactment in return. And the migration runs not only across the grid's cells but down its strata — from the surface arrangements of a life toward its enduring organizing principles, until the very terms on which a life is lived have been re-laid. The seam to the next chapter is already visible here: an apparatus that holds by itself, without anyone's enactment, is an apparatus approaching the point at which it no longer needs its users at all.
 The same migration can be read in the present tense, by watching what becomes of the two great sites of the anti-intended once the apparatus has matured. The anti-intended other — the person who will not exist, become, or act as one intends — was met, in the conventional world, by the hard work of non-intervention, the etiquette of not making trouble. In the structural world of the platform it is met differently: by the recommendation that need never surface a stranger, the block, the mute, the unmatch, the algorithm that forecloses an unwanted encounter not as a thing one declines but as a thing that never appears as an option at all. The other is managed not by tolerance but by architecture. And the anti-intended body — the body that sickens, ages, and tires against one's intention — is met at the material cells by pharmacology, the wearable, the cosmetic procedure, and at the structural-virtual cell by the quantified self, the apparatus of measurement that promises prediction and control over the very flesh, and in promising it treats the body as one more anti-intended object to be brought to heel: a disembodiment pursued, paradoxically, through ever-closer attention to the body's data.
 The undisguised statement of where this tends is transhumanism, and it is best read here, as the premise of the whole mode made explicit and unashamed.
